# The Fed's Hidden Put - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-hidden-put
> Deep inside the repo market, another round of "Not-QE" awaits

---
The liquidity drain has begun, with hundreds of billions in reserves expected to be removed from the financial system. But this reduction could not only fail to subdue risk assets. A squeeze in a relatively obscure, covert market will allow the Fed to stimulate again without a pivot. The Repo Market Put™ lies in waiting.

Recently, we’ve witnessed the full effect of the _[Transitory Pause](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-transitory-pause)_, where the Fed’s silence and inaction to tighten has caused the financial machine to send risk assets soaring. The “[TGA refill](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-transitory-pause)” has failed to bring down the most hated rally in history. Now, more tightening is needed. The U.S. government is restocking its bank account (the TGA) to pay its bills while reducing the Fed’s balance sheet via QT (quantitative tightening). If the subsequent drain of reserves is too severe, the Fed may encounter a _welcome_ surprise in the most crucial funding market.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eimT!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7360fb14-bbbf-4e74-b7b9-6d1d59430112_3674x3340.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eimT!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7360fb14-bbbf-4e74-b7b9-6d1d59430112_3674x3340.png)

The repo ([repurchase agreements](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/repurchaseagreement.asp)) market is the wheel greaser of the U.S. empire’s financial engine. Without it, U.S. dollar liquidity would be lackluster. Binding the Treasury cash and futures market together through arbitrage trades like the “futures basis” is only one of its many functions.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!rsux!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbf66eb43-2a31-4594-8fd1-0873b3cd9172_2770x1528.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!rsux!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbf66eb43-2a31-4594-8fd1-0873b3cd9172_2770x1528.png)

But above all else, repo enables “secured” lending of U.S. dollars to proliferate globally. The repo market may appear complex but it’s simply a market for connecting cash lenders like money market funds (MMFs), who must earn a yield on plain U.S. dollars, with cash borrowers like hedge funds, who need to fund leveraged positions.

It becomes clear when the _Conks_ Repo Conga™ is revealed. The repo market is a chain of market participants looking to profit from charging a spread on top of their funding costs. Cash lenders lend to “sophisticated” borrowers through dealers, who charge a spread to intermediate. In a typical conga, money market funds lend to the Fed’s primary dealers, who then lend to other securities dealers, who then lend to commercial banks and hedge funds. Repo’s end goal is to supply an endless ocean of liquidity to the financial world, and it succeeds in spectacular fashion.

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dCQf!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89e6ff12-0a64-4d5c-ab44-58105d84d3f4_630x376.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dCQf!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89e6ff12-0a64-4d5c-ab44-58105d84d3f4_630x376.png)

_Source: New York Fed_

The repo market contains multiple segments, each playing a unique role in providing liquidity. On top, [triparty repo](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2011/04/everything-you-wanted-to-know-about-the-tri-party-repo-market-but-didnt-know-to-ask/) allows cash lenders, mainly asset managers like MMFs, to lend mostly to the Fed’s primary dealers, whose job is to distribute cash to other parts of the repo market. Primary dealers then attempt to make a profit by lending these funds into “interdealer” (dealer-to-dealer) markets and charging a higher spread. Smaller securities dealers receiving these funds will then lend them on to end customers in the “bilateral” (dealer-to-customer) market for an even bigger fee. After every party’s funding needs have been met, the repo conga is complete.

At least, that’s how repo markets are supposed to operate. After recent events, its inner workings have transformed, seemingly forever. Increased market complexity and even excess liquidity have become problematic. But ironically, this will provide leaders with a concrete “Not-QE” tool to stimulate markets: the repo market put.

In September 2019, the “repocalypse” hit our screens. Money market rates and even Fed Funds, the Federal Reserve’s key policy rate, soared above its target range. The Fed’s response marked the start of a shift from an “excess collateral” to an “excess cash” regime. After attempting to reduce its balance sheet via the first official QT (quantitative tightening), the Fed performed a 180 and reinitiated QE (quantitative easing), injecting reserves into the banking system to bring rates back into range. The “cash flood” had just commenced.

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!jyGE!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7e785db6-c879-4e16-9240-6a9d0f4bcdf8_624x518.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!jyGE!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7e785db6-c879-4e16-9240-6a9d0f4bcdf8_624x518.png)

_Source: New York Fed_

Then, only months after the repocalypse and a subsequent round of QE, the COVID-19 market panic emerged. In the midst of unrivaled uncertainty, the Fed injected a colossal sum of reserves to stem illiquidity in every crucial market, from FX swaps to Eurodollars. The Fed’s monetary response eventually caused financial panic to subside, but QE endured. By 2021, reserves had grown even more in abundance. The cash flood, however, didn’t stop with the U.S. central bank. By the end of 2021 the U.S. government, after anticipating a sharp rise in welfare, had built up a record balance in its checking account, the TGA (Treasury General Account). When the government sent funds from the TGA into the banking system, this unlocked even more liquidity. “Neutralized reserves”, cash balances that cannot be deployed into the economy or financial assets, were transformed into liquid reserves and sent into the banking system, prompting banks to create deposits to balance their books. The cash flood was reaching absurd levels.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!6AhM!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3171256c-7336-4ae3-bb32-7fb7ead748ea_3730x3541.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!6AhM!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3171256c-7336-4ae3-bb32-7fb7ead748ea_3730x3541.png)

Even before all this, just after the market panic over COVID had reached maximum potency, the cash overload was already extensive, so much so that regulators were forced to allow banks to sidestep [regulatory constraints](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-dilemma) imposed on the size of their balance sheets. From the 1st of April 2020, regulators exempted U.S. Treasuries and bank reserves from the SLR (Supplementary Leverage Ratio), a regulation limiting the amount of leverage certain financial institutions can achieve. As a result, the financial behemoths of Wall Street absorbed a mammoth amount of Treasuries and bank reserves. The “cash flood” was hindered, at least for a while.

[

![US G-SIB Leverage Surcharge and Basel III Leverage Ratio](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZCP7!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg "US G-SIB Leverage Surcharge and Basel III Leverage Ratio")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZCP7!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg)

_Source: Harvard Law_

Over a year after the COVID market panic had subsided, during what was the most significant monetary mania in human history, the SLR exemption expired, causing banks to dump hundreds of billions of (now “excess”) reserves. That money had to go somewhere. After considering all options, banks started shedding reserves by disincentivizing customers from depositing i.e. charging negative rates and refusing new money. The natural location for this excess cash to end up? The Fed’s RRP (reverse repo facility).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Br6M!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7974197a-27ad-4d10-9243-f98eeb47eac2_3492x2560.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Br6M!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7974197a-27ad-4d10-9243-f98eeb47eac2_3492x2560.png)

After leverage constraints came back online in April 2021, the financial world re-assessed risk/reward and regulation. The pipes of the major financial plumbers, big banks and primary dealers, had become obstructed. Money subsequently rushed into the RRP, as investors decided MMFs were ideal. Cash investors prefer the safety of funds and liquidity over yield, so money market funds were the optimal investment. The “cash flood” was diverted into MMFs, who invested heavily in the Fed’s new “liquidity sponge” — the RRP facility.

Now, the Fed’s RRP has become the benchmark for measuring the balance between excess cash and collateral in the system. If global financial behemoths divert funds to the RRP, cash is likely in abundance. Until the RRP balance hits zero, too much money remains in the system.

In fact, cash is so ample that certain repo rates have fallen below the Fed’s ON RRP, the supposedly risk-free rate of the repo market. A medley of QE, government spending, and regulation has broken Fed’s lower boundary. Even Fed-administered rates ([TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr)/[BGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/bgcr)) — which measure repo funding costs in triparty and general collateral repo — now trade below ON RRP.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Zr-j!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F86d059c7-0a58-4fef-b270-d341db5ee16a_3896x3148.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Zr-j!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F86d059c7-0a58-4fef-b270-d341db5ee16a_3896x3148.png)

_TGCR (Triparty General Collateral Rate); dashed white line) and BGCR (Broad General Collateral Rate; solid orange line) trade below the ON RRP (yellow line)_

Since only certain entities have access to the RRP facility (some megabanks, the Fed’s primary dealers, government-sponsored enterprises like Fannie Mae, the most dominant MMFs), all other entities must accept lower rates in an excess cash regime, lending at large discounts to dealers already flushed with funding opportunities.

Until trillions in excess cash somehow disappear, marking the end of the “excess cash” era, the Fed’s RRP facility usage will remain elevated, with some repo rates dwelling below ON RRP. Liquidity today remains ample, and a repeat of 2019 is unlikely. But that could change. An attempt to return to an “excess collateral” era has begun. QT and the “TGA refill” are in effect, neutralizing reserves or removing them from the banking system completely.

Today, another repo meltdown is always possible with the intracity of the repo market, but it is less likely with the RRP full of cash. If rates begin to rise rapidly like in 2019, trillions of dollars in the RRP act as a second last line of defense against out-of-control rate spikes — and successive Fed action. Cash lenders will pull cash from the RRP to earn a higher yield, saving the Fed one extra embarrassment.

Of course, if MMFs refuse to save the day by lending in repo, the Fed’s [SRF](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2022/01/the-feds-latest-tool-a-standing-repo-facility/) (Standing Repo Facility) will become the dealer (cash lender) of last resort. This is another iteration of — yes you guessed it — “Not QE”!

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3s06!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F134a0cdd-ed16-446c-a014-1f1fadf7565d_2500x1854.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3s06!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F134a0cdd-ed16-446c-a014-1f1fadf7565d_2500x1854.png)

Unlike the 2019 repocalypse, a repo “crisis” will provide the Fed with a “Not-QE” weapon. Central bank intervention is inevitable, but their reply will be perceived as stimulative without having to cut rates or restart QE. The repo market “put” will be yet another tool to boost risk sentiment while an “official pivot” can, once again, be avoided.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for supporting macro journalism!**_

# The Fed's Plumbing Dilemma - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-plumbing-dilemma
> a speculative rush into risk assets may prompt some "plumbing QE"

---
After neutralizing trillions of dollars in excess liquidity, the Federal Reserve’s “shock absorber” has begun to decompress. Some of the $2 trillion stored in the Fed’s reverse repo facility (the RRP) is attempting to re-enter the banking system. This will not only prevent liquidity from falling to unstable levels but further threaten the U.S. central bank’s tightening stance. The Fed’s “Plumbing Dilemma” is looming.

As _Conks_ [anticipated](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-transitory-pause) last month, the U.S. central bank’s inaction and silence around its longstanding hawkish stance has caused equities to soar further, while bond yields and the U.S. dollar have, unexpectedly to most, continued to rebound. Yet again, the market has slowly started to realize the Fed’s task of dampening animal spirits will be harder than expected. One of likely many “[Transitory Pauses](https://concoda.substack.com/p/the-transitory-pause)” is now in full effect, with any emergence of recession talk marking another lower bottom in risk assets.

Once again, the Fed’s commitment to subdue animal spirits is about to be tested. Not only have risk assets [rallied](https://twitter.com/NateGeraci/status/1674870465943199767), housing starts and prices [reaccelerated](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/CSUSHPINSA), and labor market conditions remained [tight](https://twitter.com/BobEUnlimited/status/1674409721933996032?s=20), but now a large pool of excess cash has started to be drawn down by investors. Those seeking even higher yields than an alluring ~5% risk-free return have begun to pull money from [money market funds](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/m/money-marketfund.asp) (MMFs), the most cash-like investment vehicle after bank deposits. A speculative spree could be emerging.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!r0mw!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4a6c0683-e928-4e8c-b726-e2eb2e5eeb63_3316x2412.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!r0mw!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4a6c0683-e928-4e8c-b726-e2eb2e5eeb63_3316x2412.png)

Meanwhile, the much-hyped refilling of the U.S. government’s bank account — the [Treasury General Account](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/WTREGEN) (TGA) at the Fed — has passed its most perilous phase, with only $110 billion shy of the U.S. Treasury’s end-of-September target of $600 billion and liquidity remaining ample.

Trapped liquidity has been pulled from the Fed’s “shock absorber”, the RRP facility, to finance the U.S. government’s ever-increasing spending bill, while also managing to dampen the allegedly sinister effects of the “[TGA refill](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3521113a-6a5e-42a6-bbda-e71215af0a00_3774x2940.png)”. Funding the U.S. empire combined with the start of a speculative investor spree has, for the first time since May 2022, caused RRPs conducted by the Fed to drop below [$2 trillion outstanding](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/RRPONTSYD). Ultimately, the monetary plumbing has remained well-oiled throughout a period of heightened fear and uncertainty.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!y6_L!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9be3eb95-2863-417b-8cf9-2d176c21f288_5968x4723.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!y6_L!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9be3eb95-2863-417b-8cf9-2d176c21f288_5968x4723.png)

best viewed online in a browser (click the post’s title)

Money market funds financing the U.S. government by cashing out of RRPs to buy Treasuries is [liquidity neutral](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3db2cb9c-4ac1-42a5-8fc7-aaa4b6d62e56_3596x3351.png). Trapped liquidity moves straight from the Fed’s RRP facility into the U.S. government’s bank account, leaving reserves and deposits [undisturbed](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-transitory-pause). In contrast, all that’s needed for an RRP drawdown to be stimulative is if investors redeem their MMF shares, obtain bank deposits, and wander down the [risk curve](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/risk-curve.asp). The global pools of money will have travelled from cash-like safe havens to more speculative waters.

Arriving safely, however, is another question. As history shows, it will be anything but smooth sailing.

The reason the world moved into an “excess cash” era, as illustrated by the RRP absorbing a whopping $2 trillion, was — and has always been — monetary leaders changing the rules of the game, prompting the financial behemoths to rearrange their chess pieces. The global money pool then adapts to earn optimal returns within the new monetary regime. The penultimate reshuffling was forcing cash, in the form of deposits and reserves, out of the banking system into risk-free RRPs offered by the Fed _(as seen in yellow below)_.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ojZV!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe7a5c770-783a-4313-a55a-061efc402c1a_9795x10406.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ojZV!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe7a5c770-783a-4313-a55a-061efc402c1a_9795x10406.png)

best viewed online in a browser (click the post’s title)

Now, however, the latest iteration is an attempt to return to a world of “excess collateral”, with the Fed’s RRP facility declining to $0 outstanding, fading into irrelevancy once again. Ever since the Fed invented the RRP facility in 2008 to stop interest rates from falling below its target range, it has acted as a shock absorber, becoming the U.S. central bank’s go-to mechanism for quelling any disorder arising from a sudden shift in the global pools of money.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!XsOC!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd36e6b79-266e-4566-9283-c02845597c67_3048x2146.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!XsOC!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd36e6b79-266e-4566-9283-c02845597c67_3048x2146.png)

the U.S. dollar funding complex .i.e the global pools of money

In a decade characterized by our monetary leaders’ grand experiment to eradicate systemic risk from finance through a series of meaningful constraints, the Fed‘s RRP has seen its fair share of usage. In July 2014, the regulatory establishment had grown increasingly concerned over the stability of MMFs. Because during the GFC (Great Financial Crisis) it [took](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/feds/files/2022012pap.pdf) the failure of just one fund sponsor to provoke a run on the entire MMF complex, regulators vowed to stem the effects of a 2008-style replay.

By introducing an extensive overhaul of the MMF industry, monetary authorities triggered a significant reshuffling of the global pools of money. The SEC (Securities and Exchange Commission) made shares in prime MMFs — which hold fewer sovereign debt-based instruments than government money market funds — less “deposit-like” by allowing redemption gates and fees, while making it [harder](https://mutualfunds.com/education/floating-nav-for-money-market-funds/) for prime funds to achieve a stable NAV, jargon for MMF shares equaling $1 per share. From then on, only MMFs filled with government paper retained money-like attributes similar to bank deposits. In response, investors seeking to preserve liquidity pulled around $1.2 trillion from prime MMFs and rotated into their government counterparts.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cUYg!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fac4f155d-5ff3-4ed8-b707-af4c3733631e_2226x1896.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cUYg!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fac4f155d-5ff3-4ed8-b707-af4c3733631e_2226x1896.png)

Source: BIS

Overnight, monetary leaders had effectively deemed some forms of money as “excess”, a euphemism for cash that remained unallocated as a result of regulation — plus risk versus reward. As money market participants adjusted to this new constraint, the Fed’s shock absorber was reactivated. Now awash with cash and unable to source enough investments compatible with the new regime, MMFs piled into RRPs offered by the Fed. Subsequently, the RRP facility had prevented any turmoil from emerging in the money market complex.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!EvW_!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fecb9affd-c03b-49f2-bc58-4a157961d8dc_768x576.jpeg)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!EvW_!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fecb9affd-c03b-49f2-bc58-4a157961d8dc_768x576.jpeg)

Source: New York Fed

By 2018, after the financial behemoths fully adapted, the MMF complex had sourced enough assets to invest in that RRPs at the Fed fell to near-zero. Another “excess cash” era had concluded.

But that was only until COVID-19 changed the monetary landscape once more. As shown in _[The Fed’s Hidden Put](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-hidden-put),_ regulators temporarily permitted banks to hold as many reserves and Treasuries as they needed (known as an SLR exemption) to not only make markets but settle payments during the COVID market panic. Then, almost a year after the chaos subsided, monetary leaders ripped off the band-aid by forcing banks to once again abide by the SLR leverage ratio. Banks, however, could no longer hold every deposit on their balance sheets. Another “excess cash” era was born.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!DjkD!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff725141f-997e-4379-a495-ce9d3da24131_2619x1920.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!DjkD!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff725141f-997e-4379-a495-ce9d3da24131_2619x1920.png)

Fast forward to today, a combination of the Fed [flooding](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/TOTRESNS) the system with reserves, and the Treasury slowing its issuance of government debt while [drawing down](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/WTREGEN) its TGA balance from record highs, has formed an excess cash pool topping two trillion dollars. This amount resides in, none other than, the Fed’s shock absorber.

Now, though, the Fed’s RRP balance has begun a prolonged struggle to return to $0 outstanding. The recent speculative investor spree is likely the start, driving money out of MMFs and into the banking system, removing the need for MMFs to lend to the Fed via RRPs. Yet the banking system cannot accommodate all that money. If too much “excess cash” leaves the RRP in search of higher returns — and outweighs other dynamics such as QT — authorities could be compelled to exempt reserves from its leverage ratio (another SLR exemption), allowing banks to absorb more reserves and deposits. Relieving banks of balance sheet constraints will likely be perceived by markets as yet another variety of “Not-QE”, working against the Fed’s supposed tightening. The Fed’s Plumbing Dilemma™ could be another “stealth QE” boost for markets. One that could be about to transpire.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks subscriber!**_

# The Coming Volatility Suppression - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression
> the Fed's primary bailout mechanism is growing more systemic

---
The most noteworthy transformation of the global monetary system is almost complete. By shifting from an [unsecured](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/l/libor.asp) to a [secured](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-rise-of-the-secured-standard) standard, monetary leaders have attempted to eliminate systemic risk. But risk has only been transferred, not eliminated. The Federal Reserve’s Volatility Suppressor™ is about to be unleashed.

After the bursting of the subprime bubble in 2008, a paradigm shift in banking unfolded. While banks suddenly no longer wanted to lend to each other or bear excessive risk, the global regulatory complex set out to prevent a repeat of the crisis. The demise of the “unsecured standard” had commenced. Central bankers flooded the system with enough reserves to kill the [Fed Funds](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) market, the market for unsecured interbank loans. Meanwhile, the regulatory establishment had begun to devise an exhaustive array of measures to discourage unsecured lending. Dubbed the [Basel III Accords](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/b/basell-iii.asp#:~:text=Basel%20III%20is%20an%20international,being%20implemented%20as%20of%202022.) by its architects at the [BIS](https://www.bis.org/about/index.htm?m=1001) (Bank of International Settlements), a new global banking standard had been born.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VtLD!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff49135e4-d349-496e-8dab-ed07ac0cfcef_3216x2224.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VtLD!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff49135e4-d349-496e-8dab-ed07ac0cfcef_3216x2224.png)

Known collectively as “the agencies,” the Federal Reserve Board, FDIC (Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation), and OCC (The Office of the Comptroller of the Currency) not only adopted the BIS’s (Bank of International Settlements) new banking standard, however, but went a step further. By April 2014, each agency had fully enforced an [additional](https://www.federalregister.gov/documents/2014/04/14/2014-08259/regulatory-capital-rules-regulatory-capital-implementation-of-basel-iii-capital-adequacy-transition) set of constraints, obliterating the concept of banks taking on excessive risk forever. From then on, the Wall Street behemoths had to raise enormous amounts of capital to execute — what used to be — even the simplest of trades. Any dealing deemed mildly speculative by monetary officials had been outlawed from the banking system.

By doing so, America’s monetary leaders had not only forced banks out of risky trades but also forced the largest market makers to [pull back](https://www.alliancebernstein.com/corporate/en/insights/investment-insights/whats-behind-the-drop-in-treasury-market-liquidity.html) from providing liquidity in key areas of the financial system. With the “shadow banks”, [non-bank financial institutions](https://www.worldbank.org/en/publication/gfdr/gfdr-2016/background/nonbank-financial-institution#:~:text=Examples%20of%20nonbank%20financial%20institutions,specialize%20in%20sectors%20or%20groups.) not subject to the same stringent regulations, successfully filling in the gaps, the regulatory war against banks’ risk-taking abilities reached escape velocity. The Global Regulatory Complex™ achieved its most sought-after objective.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KttI!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9ecfa5d1-e156-4ec8-ac00-7938584790c1_5958x5674.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!KttI!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9ecfa5d1-e156-4ec8-ac00-7938584790c1_5958x5674.png)

The Global Regulatory Complex: Each global and regional regulation explained in plain English (best viewed in a browser)

Slowly but surely, over the past decade, the banking arm of every global financial goliath has been transformed into nothing more than a boring utility, alleviating itself of its most speculative risk-taking capabilities. Even so, following the Silicon Valley Bank [collapse](https://www.techtarget.com/whatis/feature/Silicon-Valley-Bank-collapse-explained-What-you-need-to-know) and subsequent regional banking panic, regulators have once again decided to reignite their tenacity. In the face of a shocked and puzzled finance industry, authorities are about to force banks to implement even harsher capital requirements — raising the amount of liquid capital that must be held against their assets. Some estimates [suggest](https://www.ft.com/content/e8dff41b-6854-4759-b69c-97b4eea21929) the largest Wall Street banks will have to raise their capital “buffers” by a whopping 24%.

Swiftly, it’s become clear that the regulatory war on bank risk-taking is far from over. Yet what’s also evident is the effects this ongoing battle will have on global finance. No matter how extreme, the upcoming constraints will push even more activity away from banks and toward the shadow banking layer. A subsequent increase in the opaqueness of the financial system will ensue, prompting an even greater reliance on the Fed’s volatility suppressor to come to the rescue when choppy waters emerge. How will the U.S. central bank’s primary bailout mechanism save the day? It’s more than just a confidence booster. Let’s dive in.

The unique, contemporary effort by regulators to rid the global monetary system of systemic risk has been defined by a move away from “bank-based” finance and toward “market-based” alternatives. While “bank-based” finance refers to bank-based operations, “market-based” finance refers mainly to shadow banks (such as money market funds and securities dealers) and the markets (such as debt and equity) in which they operate. Under the secured standard, banks now stick to their more traditional functions. Shadow banks, meanwhile, absorb any excess demand that the Global Regulatory Complex™ has denied prominent participants from fulfilling. The most glaring sign of this evolution has been large dealers’ declining appetite for making markets, even in the most pristine of collateral: U.S. Treasuries.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Bc8x!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F33c42c8a-285a-4864-861f-e2c25d5cc18d_5366x4246.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Bc8x!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F33c42c8a-285a-4864-861f-e2c25d5cc18d_5366x4246.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

A decade ago, the Fed’s [primary dealers](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/primarydealers) — a select group of dealer banks and broker-dealers mandated by the U.S. central bank to make markets in Treasuries — were once able to absorb up to 80% of every 10-year Treasury auction. Now, however, exposed to the Fed’s SLR ([supplementary leverage ratio](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/w_1456,c_limit,f_webp,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg)) and the Fed’s tougher restrictions imposed on systemically important participants, primary dealers absorb on average a mere ~17% of bond issuance at auctions. When you’re constrained under Basel III, avoiding business activities that yield small profits, such as making markets in Treasuries, has grown necessary. Superseding the former major players are the traders and asset managers, who directly bid for U.S. government securities themselves at auctions via online platforms. Perhaps not so coincidentally, the software that made this all possible was released during the crisis that spurred Basel III’s creation.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qHnf!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d28784d-9971-4f40-98f3-7872dfdfd1bf_1200x675.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qHnf!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d28784d-9971-4f40-98f3-7872dfdfd1bf_1200x675.png)

The most relevant change emanating from the move to market-based finance, however, was the rapid growth of the repo market, the market for [secured cash loans](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/w_1456,c_limit,f_webp,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F90bae932-293c-4473-a085-9cf47f7be4e0_2781x2388.png). Other debt and equity markets, such as corporate debt, ballooned in volume. Still, since repos were loans primarily secured by high-quality sovereign debt, the repo market prospered. What’s more, the abundant liquidity of the dollar funding markets meant that if entities ever fell short of U.S. Treasuries, they could simply tap the [securities lending markets](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-rise-of-the-secured-standard), temporarily swapping lower-quality debt securities for Treasuries to participate.

In their models, America’s monetary leaders plus the rest of the Global Regulatory Complex assumed U.S. Treasuries, the collateral powering the secured standard, could weather any storm. But they were swiftly proven wrong: the COVID-19 market turmoil had arrived. The 2008 credit crunch was defined by a dash for safe assets, but the COVID-19 market crisis was defined by a dash for cold, hard dollars. Being the closest alternative to cash, U.S. Treasuries were liquidated en masse. Bond market illiquidity arose.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!mHfP!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F11bd4b9c-84a7-4ec2-bae1-ce2506ebf066_1725x1136.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!mHfP!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F11bd4b9c-84a7-4ec2-bae1-ce2506ebf066_1725x1136.png)

_Source: BNY Mellon_

By committing to [purchase](https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/pressreleases/monetary20200315a.htm) virtually unlimited amounts of U.S. Treasuries, the Fed’s “volatility suppressor” was born. U.S. sovereign debt was shown to only be truly risk-free when paired with added monetary alchemy and intervention from the Federal Reserve. Extensive central bank intervention was the only solution to stabilizing the value of “safe assets” backing trillions of dollars in funding transactions. The idea that securing more loans against sovereign debt delivered complete stability had been crushed. Only with the Fed’s “volatility suppressor” could confidence in the financial system be resurrected.

Next time, it will be no different. When another inevitable black swan surfaces, the Fed’s playbook remains unchanged. During future crises, the forthcoming stricter regulations — the so-called _Basel III Endgame_ — will only put more pressure on the Fed to fire up its volatility suppressor to restore stability in opaque markets where shadow banks operate. Whether or not monetary leaders have to abandon their unofficial “stealth easing” stance for official easing (QE and rate cuts) is now the more fundamental question.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Dollar Swap Network - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame?utm_source=publication-search
> the Federal Reserve's dollar swap network is set to expand

---
Despite the Federal Reserve’s stealth easing programs persisting, dollar strength has begun to accelerate once again. But as liquidity subsequently drains, the world’s response will not be to de-dollarize but to seek more dollar liquidity. The U.S. central bank’s global swap network is set to expand.

Back in 2019, before the Fed unleashed trillions of dollars in liquidity to stem COVID fears, global markets had entered a lengthy “excess collateral” era. The Fed’s RRP (reverse repo) facility, a shock absorber for excess cash and the defacto measure of surplus liquidity, lay empty. Almost every dollar was being deployed by private sector entities into trades that did not alter the Fed’s balance sheet, from [repos](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-unwind) funding leveraged Treasury investors to [Eurodollar](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-eurodollar-system) arbitrage by New York branches of foreign banks. Soon, however, a relatively unknown trade was not only about to upend the excess collateral era but also the illusion of financial stability in the Fed’s [post-crisis](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-federal-reserve-endgame-is-not) system.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-3YL!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5b403c84-d30e-4232-8718-99bf4a2ce659_7043x4980.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-3YL!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5b403c84-d30e-4232-8718-99bf4a2ce659_7043x4980.png)

Midway through September 2019 on the 17th, a funding squeeze [caused](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fec1ca36f-2e30-4175-915f-f643f74f828a_624x518.png) money market rates to skyrocket. The Fed’s [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr) (Secured Overnight Financing Rate), a measure of the average cost to borrow cash secured against U.S. Treasuries, showed repo rates spiking above a whopping 5%. Meanwhile, in a frantic search for other sources of dollar funding, financial entities had even begun tapping the once-lifeless [interbank funding market](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-death-of-the-interbank-market), sending the Fed Funds rate ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)) above the U.S. central bank’s upper limit of its target range.

[

![Image](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9Guv!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4499dd1-fd82-48cb-ba63-00789648d543_800x574.png "Image")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9Guv!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4499dd1-fd82-48cb-ba63-00789648d543_800x574.png)

Source: New York Fed

The Fed responded with a [Standing Repo Facility](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/repo-agreement-ops-faq), and shortly after, the “repocalypse” subsided. But the central bank’s bottomless interventions were no longer the major astonishment. The system was not as stable as monetary leaders and prominent financial players had assumed. It was soon revealed that most market players knew on the day of the repocalypse that liquidity was about to grow scarcer than usual. Days before, most were aware that corporations would be pulling large sums of cash from MMFs (money market funds) to fund quarterly tax payments, while the U.S. Treasury would be issuing additional government securities into an increasingly illiquid market. Both would pull liquidity — in the form of reserves — from the banking system into the [TGA](https://www.fiscal.treasury.gov/tga/), the government’s bank account housed inside the Federal Reserve System.

At the same time, those same financial actors had been assigning large quantities of reserves to trades in the repo market. While dealers increased their repo borrowing to a point where their inventories — and thus their balance sheets — had reached near capacity, lending in repo [became](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/w_1456,c_limit,f_webp,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa3e202af-7c21-40c7-81aa-5f26138c91f4_2601x1824.png) so profitable that banks made more deploying reserves into repos than depositing these at the Fed and earning [IOER](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/reserve-balances.htm) (interest on excess reserves). These decisions were also made in the depths of the Fed’s first official QT (quantitative tightening) program, which aimed to drain billions in reserves from the financial system every month. All in all, the late-2019 repo market had become a ticking timebomb.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!WsYX!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ab779a2-943a-45a0-9c7d-3555c9bf8fb9_5359x5241.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!WsYX!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ab779a2-943a-45a0-9c7d-3555c9bf8fb9_5359x5241.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

Yet in the aftermath and throughout the repocalypse, another market undergoing mild stress had barely been covered in the media: the $4 trillion-a-day FX swap market. As smaller participants failed to locate funding both in repo and the interbank markets, the rate to borrow dollars secured against foreign currencies spiked, though only slightly. But it was merely a few months after the repo spike faded from mainstream circles that we’d not only discover what real stress in the FX swap market looked like. The world would also come to acknowledge the Fed’s global dollar swap network as the FX swap market’s sole rescue mechanism in times of crisis. The Covid financial panic had emerged, and with it came swap lines.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!u1-G!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb2134a66-3146-45fc-a8f4-7f02bfbc4380_1006x639.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!u1-G!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb2134a66-3146-45fc-a8f4-7f02bfbc4380_1006x639.png)

bid-ask spreads in FX swaps during the repo spike | Source: NBER

As pandemic fears spread, every one of the 25+ major dollar funding markets began to seize up, including the four-trillion-dollar-a-day FX swap goliath. Market participants worldwide, looking to source dollars for currency hedging and liquidity needs, suddenly had to pay a premium to enter into FX swap contracts, the near equivalent of “currency repos”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!UIQ6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffb305b7c-6317-46d7-9bd2-a77e957ff585_990x1032.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!UIQ6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffb305b7c-6317-46d7-9bd2-a77e957ff585_990x1032.png)

FX bid-ask spreads during the COVID market meltdown | Source: IMF

In a currency repo (short for [repurchase agreement](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdf6f675f-fe9c-482e-85ae-1783a892bbc5_2781x2388.png)), two parties swap currencies at the present (spot) exchange rate while agreeing at a later date to reverse the swap at the present forward rate. This allows participants to lock in today’s exchange rates for a fee, protecting themselves from adverse (but also favorable) currency moves. While standard repos are classed as on-balance sheet assets and liabilities, currency repos are recorded off-balance sheet — as payments can only occur at the maturity of the swap contract.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PQSm!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd0b103b1-acca-4987-aad4-9bb110880cb9_7087x4632.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!PQSm!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd0b103b1-acca-4987-aad4-9bb110880cb9_7087x4632.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

The COVID-induced funding stress was so extensive, however, that the “upper hierarchy” of the U.S. dollar swap network was activated. Those unable to source dollars via their usual venues of repo and Eurodollar markets called upon currency repos to secure liquidity. But they soon discovered that even in FX swaps, dollars had grown scarce. Their usual FX dealers were unable to provide the goods. They had to go elsewhere.

Next, they turned to the New York branches of foreign banks, “the private dealers of first resort”. In non-crisis periods when markets grew imbalanced, foreign banks subject to lax regulation and with greater access to dollar funding stepped in to make markets in FX swaps. Still, despite these trades now yielding greater profits via wider bid-ask spreads, foreign banks pulled back, not wanting to expose themselves to market volatility that would breach internal protocols.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gALZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb83a5c98-d485-4216-a0dc-16ebe8316529_5466x5855.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gALZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb83a5c98-d485-4216-a0dc-16ebe8316529_5466x5855.png)

the color scale denotes the severity of dollar imbalances

The next avenue for dollar liquidity seekers was to tap the Wall Street megabanks: the private dealers of _last_ resort. Yet, they got the same response. Despite spreads widening enough to attract large U.S. banks repressed by [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm) regulations, the Wall Street goliaths also pulled back from FX swap market making. Internal risk limits and [regulatory constraints](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa45fc144-56b1-465b-a9db-3630f405c80d_864x385.jpeg) meant that even JPMorgan and its large hoard of reserves were no match for COVID-19’s volatile conditions.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2yPH!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fecfb1895-a6b0-4521-8116-72b52025ed28_5168x5117.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2yPH!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fecfb1895-a6b0-4521-8116-72b52025ed28_5168x5117.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

Now that the ultimate private dealer of last resort was hindered, the public dealer of last resort was primed and ready to step in. The Federal Reserve’s dollar swap lines were about to be activated. On March 15, 2020, FX swap markets broke down completely. In no time at all, the Federal Reserve along with other G7 central banks [announced](https://www.bankofengland.co.uk/news/2020/march/coordinated-central-bank-action-to-enhance-the-provision-of-global-us-dollar-liquidity) the resurgence of U.S. dollar swap lines.

By offering to provide unlimited liquidity at its “upper global jaw,” which cost OIS (the overnight indexed swap rate) plus 0.25%, the Fed caused volatility to subside. The private dealers of first and last resort returned, bringing the FX swaps market back into balance. Prominent FX swap dealers in [G7 countries](https://www.google.com/search?q=g7+countries&rlz=1C5CHFA_enGB930GB930&oq=G7+countries&gs_lcrp=EgZjaHJvbWUqDAgAEAAYQxixAxiKBTIMCAAQABhDGLEDGIoFMgcIARAAGIAEMgcIAhAAGIAEMgcIAxAAGIAEMgcIBBAAGIAEMgcIBRAAGIAEMgcIBhAAGIAEMgcIBxAAGIAEMgcICBAAGIAEMgcICRAAGIAE0gEHMzI5ajBqN6gCALACAA&sourceid=chrome&ie=UTF-8), from Japan to Canada, cried out to their regional central banks for dollar liquidity, who in turn called up the Fed, creating an intricate web of dollar loans and claims. The FX swap bailout was complete.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nCYs!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0f4f5e7f-f6a4-4517-aa0d-d143a7c06c99_5081x5062.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nCYs!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F0f4f5e7f-f6a4-4517-aa0d-d143a7c06c99_5081x5062.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

At the time, the dollar had already secured its spot as the dominant global currency, making up half of all trade invoices, cross-border loans, and international debt securities. 88% of all FX trades involved the Greenback, while ~59% of all foreign exchange reserves were dollar-denominated. The FX swap lines and their effectiveness during COVID simply added an extra layer to the world’s dollar dependency, along with that of the other 25 comprehensive funding markets — like repo and Eurodollars, none of which have run into compelling rivals. The vast majority of countries and nations are no longer using the global dollar system: they are part of it, acting as connectors to bridge trillion-dollar imbalances in global markets daily, without central banks and governments ever intervening.

But when no one else comes to the rescue, the only backup monetary leaders have developed is the Fed’s dollar swap network. What’s more, they are becoming more attached to it, deploying liquidity lines swifter than ever before. In the recent collapse of Silicon Valley Bank and the bailout of Credit Suisse, swap lines were activated at the first sign of hazard. So when another monetary emergency strikes, the same swift response is assured. The golden age of the Federal Reserve acting as “the swapper of last resort” looks to have only just begun.

_**If you liked this, feel free to hit the ♡ button to let us know and share a link via social media to help us grow. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Final Frontier - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-final-frontier
> a recent central bank invention has tightened the dollar's grip

---
While news of the U.S. dollar losing its supremacy has been spreading quickly, behind the scenes, America’s monetary leaders have been taking steps to reinforce the Greenback’s global hegemony. Now, with the Federal Reserve’s “final frontier” in operation, the dollar’s grip is about to grow stronger than ever.

Since the Great Financial Crisis (GFC) of 2008, the Federal Reserve has operated a system, not built on sound pre-planning, but on its responses to a seemingly endless number of crises in increasingly complex markets. Its decision to flood the financial system with bank reserves shattered the [existing mechanism](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-federal-reserve-endgame-is-not) for setting interest rates, sending Fed officials on a journey to implement a fresh monetary regime: The price of money (interest rates) would no longer be set by the Fed altering the number of reserves in the system but by implementing a set of rates. The result? The gradual emergence of the Fed’s “global jaws”.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!JJ4E!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F827ab014-72c4-4a2e-94cb-9e33ce0250ca_6721x5331.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!JJ4E!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F827ab014-72c4-4a2e-94cb-9e33ce0250ca_6721x5331.png)

the Fed’s global jaws (i.e. global rates levels/corridor system)

First came the Fed’s initial upper jaw in 2008. On October 6th, shortly after the fall of Lehman Brothers, the Fed started paying interest on reserve account balances, most of which belonged to the big Wall Street banks. Named [IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB) or “interest on reserve balances”, this rate acted as an anchor on Fed Funds, holding it — and other short-term money market rates — within the Fed’s newly established target range (_as seen below in navy blue_).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VPh3!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fcf313ccc-a2e2-487f-87ed-e17b6fb4ca87_5724x4578.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!VPh3!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fcf313ccc-a2e2-487f-87ed-e17b6fb4ca87_5724x4578.png)

the Fed’s local jaws (latest domestic money market rates)

Though a significant change, paying interest on bank reserves was just the start of the Fed’s jaw construction. Soon after, officials began assembling a soft lower jaw: a tool to nudge falling money market rates, including Fed Funds ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)) and short-term Treasury yields, back into its target range. In late 2013, with both rates and yields near zero and approaching negative territory, Fed officials grew worried, not only about yields on Treasuries turning negative, but that when the Fed hiked for the first time since the GFC, raising the IORB rate (the Fed’s upper jaw) was sufficient to drive short-term rates higher. The Fed reacted swiftly, implementing an overnight reverse repo facility, commonly known as [the RRP](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fcf50fd6b-b7f5-4948-86e2-ecf866ea12e1_2597x1819.png). This allowed certain banks, money market funds, and GSEs (government-sponsored enterprises, like Fannie Mae and Freddie Mac) to lend to the Fed at its overnight repo rate (ON RRP). By increasing ON RRP, the Fed could raise short-term rates whenever it deemed necessary. Its soft lower jaw was now in operation.

The initial versions of the Fed’s upper and lower jaws, however, were defective and incomplete. Still to surface were various disruptive episodes, which would come to remold their initial structures. After the infamous “[repocalypse](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame)” of September 2019, the Fed introduced the standing repo facility ([SRF](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1848fffa-6ef4-4d36-9a5a-7c21db373709_2500x1854.png)), setting a hard ceiling on repo rates. Entities borrowed cash at the standing repo rate (SRFR), bypassing higher private borrowing costs, which restored balance in markets. A few years later, the COVID market meltdown showed that the Fed required a permanent global backstop. Monetary leaders reintroduced official central bank [swap lines](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame), fortifying the Fed’s upper jaws. Still, the Fed’s job wasn’t done.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!t1T7!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffea996e4-5163-4bbf-9b48-d20a4ff50dc7_7627x6326.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!t1T7!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ffea996e4-5163-4bbf-9b48-d20a4ff50dc7_7627x6326.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

The volatility of COVID-19 was so extreme that it threatened the stability of public institutions’ dollar portfolios. Struggling with a volatile atmosphere, foreign official institutions (FOIs) — namely the treasuries and central banks of foreign governments plus international organizations — were on the brink of firesaling U.S. Treasuries and other dollar assets to raise cold, hard cash. Before a governmental-level calamity ensued, the Fed stepped in, adding another layer to its global jaws. The [FIMA](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/fima-repo-facility.htm) repo facility, the Fed’s “final frontier”, was born.

Alongside the private dollar ceiling of [swap lines](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame), a public dollar ceiling was about to provide America’s allies and rivals with another safety mechanism in times of crisis. Yet it would also increase the world’s reliance on the U.S. dollar and its many derivatives. What did this look like in practice? Let’s go deeper into the mechanics.

Since the rise of its post-WWII golden age, America has produced the deepest capital markets on record, the assets of which global finance has eagerly devoured. Meanwhile, via its treasury and central bank, the U.S. has also provided a range of investments exclusively for governmental entities worldwide. The most significant by far has been the introduction of the Fed’s foreign repo pool (FRP), a facility designed to cater to finance ministries, treasuries, and central banks of foreign nations.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-ykb!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F054fa11d-e7da-4f34-901d-ad7bae35d360_7627x6326.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-ykb!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F054fa11d-e7da-4f34-901d-ad7bae35d360_7627x6326.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed in a browser)

Upon its inception in the 1970s, the FRP has offered FX reserve managers of foreign governments a risk-free investment opportunity, helping them to meet their dollar liquidity needs. FRPs, repos conducted through the foreign repo pool, are secured dollar loans, where FOIs invest end-of-day cash balances in their master accounts at the U.S. central bank overnight, secured against Treasuries from the Fed’s asset portfolio: the [SOMA](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/soma-holdings) (System Open Market Account). The Fed’s SOMA managers sell securities to FOIs, agreeing to buy back these assets at a slightly higher price — a price that the Fed adjusts without ample notice.

This process has enabled the Fed to serve as a “public borrower of last resort”, preventing money market stress created through FOIs falling short of securities to invest in. The FRP achieves this by providing public institutions with an alternative investment for their excess dollars: RRPs (reverse repos) backed by securities from its vast asset portfolio. Because the U.S. central bank is deemed the safest dollar counterparty, the rate charged at the FRP (FRPR) forms a _public_ dollar rate floor, much like the _private_ dollar rate floor (ON RRP) set at the Fed’s regular (and separate) [RRP facility](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/domestic-market-operations/monetary-policy-implementation/repo-reverse-repo-agreements).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1P3m!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe59dfcd2-bad0-44d4-828e-7985bff7a9dd_5655x4219.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1P3m!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe59dfcd2-bad0-44d4-828e-7985bff7a9dd_5655x4219.png)

Only in late 2015 had the FRP experienced a sharp increase in usage, after the Fed started paying a higher yield to those willing to park their dollars overnight at the facility. Since the return on lending cash via the FRP exceeded yields on Treasury bills, money primarily from foreign central banks began pouring in. Then, after leveling out for years, the FRP rose again following the aftermath of the COVID-19 market panic. And the trend has continued upward.

Little did the FRP know, however, that it would soon have an opposing twin. While the FRP had acted alone as the Fed’s lower frontier (.i.e the public dollar floor) for decades, finally the Fed’s upper frontier (the public dollar ceiling) was about to be birthed. All it took was, say, the volatile conditions of a global pandemic.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!41PX!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe092a91b-669e-4359-91f8-9f173e8c18d2_6721x5331.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!41PX!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe092a91b-669e-4359-91f8-9f173e8c18d2_6721x5331.png)

Amidst extreme stress in both Treasury and dollar funding markets during the COVID hysteria, the U.S. central bank responded swiftly. On the 31st of March 2020, the Fed assembled the Foreign and International Monetary Authorities (FIMA) repo facility. By offering secured cash loans to central banks and other foreign governmental entities, the FIMA facility prevented both America’s allies and foes from ditching their major dollar investments. Instead of outright selling, they could temporarily swap their U.S. Treasuries for U.S. dollar liquidity — in the form of reserves.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!FdtW!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7202ce76-bb84-4752-8aa4-33ee1edf6ecf_7627x6326.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!FdtW!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7202ce76-bb84-4752-8aa4-33ee1edf6ecf_7627x6326.png)

the same graphic as above; added for convenience

In a run on U.S. assets, America’s closest rivals were more determined to raise dollars than reduce their exposure to the Greenback altogether. What’s more, China — America’s largest rival — was likely the largest user of the Fed’s final frontier, right as the dollar system grew most unstable. Rather than ditching the dollar hegemony, their priorities were not only to obtain more dollar liquidity but to provide it to desperate regional banks and other financial players simultaneously.

Escaping Uncle Sam requires ditching the Greenback. But a modern-day exodus will result in most of its allies and, more importantly, its rivals demanding more dollars. The Fed’s final frontier, with its power to easily deter sales of U.S. assets in times of crisis, only makes a non-dollar-centric world even harder to accomplish.

# The Fed's New Rescue Mechanism - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-rescue-mechanism
> the central bank's emergency facility is about to evolve

---
After a year of neutralizing losses on banks’ underwater assets, the Federal Reserve’s [BTFP](https://www.federalreserve.gov/financial-stability/bank-term-funding-program.htm) (Bank Term Funding Program) is about to expire. A facility once used to quash financial panic and stimulate markets has been abused by the very banks it aimed to safeguard. Subsequently, the U.S. central bank is not only about to commence an “emergency phaseout” but assemble a new version of its primary rescue mechanism.

In March 2023, the remnants of COVID-19’s speculative boom came back to haunt the banks and monetary leaders that enabled it. The failure of Fed officials and Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) executives to address the hazards of handling a vast amount of flighty deposits ended in a string of bank failures. After SVB declared an emergency restructuring on March 8th, its heavily concentrated VC investor base engaged in a coordinated exodus, pulling billions of dollars in uninsured bank deposits from the Silicon Valley giant. Following the spread of rumors on and off social media the next day, another $100 billion in deposits (and thus reserves) was set to flee SVB’s balance sheet. Since obtaining more reserves to process further customer outflows required selling bonds heavily devalued by the Fed’s rate hikes, SVB’s equity cushion was set to be wiped out. Realizing they were toast, SVB executives contacted regulators, who promptly shuttered the bank’s doors on March 10th. Still, news of SVB’s collapse paired with Silvergate Bank’s [earlier demise](https://www.forbes.com/sites/brianbushard/2023/03/08/crypto-bank-silvergate-will-shut-down-amid-financial-problems/) initiated a run on the regional banking system. By Friday’s market close, a mammoth Fed intervention was looming.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!D7Yv!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F63c04eda-c689-49ba-9eb9-80dc0bffdf76_7217x6918.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!D7Yv!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F63c04eda-c689-49ba-9eb9-80dc0bffdf76_7217x6918.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Upon resolving numerous failed banks and broadcasting further potential actions to calm the public, monetary leaders made their most assertive move to prevent financial turmoil from spreading. On March 12th, just before markets opened following a nervy weekend, the Fed [announced](https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/pressreleases/monetary20240124a.htm) the opening of its largest confidence booster: the Bank Term Funding Program, or BTFP for short. By enabling banks and other depository institutions to borrow cash (in the form of reserves) equaling the full ([par](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/p/parvalue.asp)) value of their securities — not the market value exposed to losses from rising interest rates, the Fed effectively bailed out banks’ interest rate (duration) risk.

Although this only applied to banks’ asset portfolios, the U.S. central bank indirectly signaled to market participants that it was willing to neutralize any amount of duration risk to maintain stability. Minus a tiny blip involving the long-awaited demise of investment banking giant, Credit Suisse, the Fed’s measures reignited sentiment, sparking a durable rally in risk assets. Quantitative Teasing™ had been unleashed.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Xujs!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F27eab136-de0a-4cf2-bb09-3839943c5165_6907x5589.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Xujs!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F27eab136-de0a-4cf2-bb09-3839943c5165_6907x5589.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

As it happened, market participants correctly assessed the strength of the Fed’s interventions. Only a few poorly managed banks collapsed, while every other bank facing a squeeze turned to the BTFP for assistance. Increasing volumes at the Fed’s facility [stoked](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/H41RESPPALDKNWW) fears that the banking turmoil had yet to conclude. But a few months later, the rise in BTFP loans outstanding began to [slow](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/H41RESPPALDKNWW), peaking at around $107 billion. As economic growth remained resilient and inflation rose at a slower pace, risk assets kept advancing, until [increased issuance](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-stealth-liquidity-squeeze) of longer-dated sovereign debt in Q3 provoked disruption in financial markets. The Treasury, however, soon performed a 180 and eased coupon issuance, marking the start of one of the sharpest equity rallies ever witnessed. By December 2023, the Fed started to deliver clear signals of rate cuts ahead. The coast finally seemed clear for the banking system.

That was until volumes at the BTFP facility reaccelerated. But yet again, fears were misplaced. Increased usage delivered another false signal of impending ruin. Because Fed officials chose 1-year OIS (the 1-year overnight indexed swap rate, a forward-looking interbank borrowing measure) plus 0.10% as the cost to borrow from the facility, the BTFP rate plunged while several cuts were priced in. The BTFP consequently [transformed](https://pbs.twimg.com/media/GCXM4dPXUAAHriO?format=jpg&name=medium) from an emergency backstop into a risk-free arbitrage for banks, who started borrowing reserves cheaply from the facility to “lend” to the Fed at a higher rate of interest. This involved banks depositing reserves borrowed from the BTFP into their Fed master accounts to earn interest on reserve balances ([IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB)), profiting from roughly a 0.50% (50bps) spread.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!o-Zz!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F315d1a46-bde2-4b7e-908f-8ab0b1a79e9c_5451x5070.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!o-Zz!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F315d1a46-bde2-4b7e-908f-8ab0b1a79e9c_5451x5070.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Week after week, alongside a favorable economic outlook and some of the loosest financial conditions on record, arbitrage trades pushed BTFP volumes higher, [peaking](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/H41RESPPALDKNWW) at around $170 billion outstanding. Then, on January 25th, 2024, the Fed suddenly revealed it would no longer accept new BTFP loans after the facility expires on March 11th, 2024. The U.S. central bank also killed the BTFP arbitrage, raising the facility’s minimum borrowing rate to equal IORB. This meant banks would earn 0% (0bps) on future trades.

Fast forward to today, the Fed and other monetary leaders globally have begun to fix the apparent flaws in their “lender of last resort” systems, the same systems in which the BTFP was established. Plans to construct an updated version of the Fed’s primary rescue mechanism are now underway. Let’s dive deeper.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eVBm!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F532e0c15-a6b0-40f1-91be-51c4e537d3fe_7041x5088.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eVBm!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F532e0c15-a6b0-40f1-91be-51c4e537d3fe_7041x5088.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

When U.S. monetary leaders announced the BTFP update, it became clear that shortly after the banking panic had subsided, most banks taking out emergency loans were liquidity-constrained but nowhere near insolvency. After all, to be eligible for BTFP loans, the bank requesting reserves must offer the Fed proof that they’re in sound financial condition. Allowing the BTFP to expire was confirmation that ([almost](https://www.firstrepublic.com/)) all banks requesting “emergency” funding remained in one piece. Moreover, after the dust settled, banks likely took immediate steps to boost their resilience. By attracting deposits — once lost during outflows — to raise reserves while increasing their levels of capital, banks provided themselves a greater margin of safety against another squeeze emerging.

Following the Fed’s latest changes, arbitrage may have been eliminated, yet the BTFP and its plumbing remain unchanged, continuing to accept new loans until the facility expires on March 11th. For a bank to receive BTFP funding, it must not only prove its solvency but be “operationally ready” to access the [Primary Credit Facility](https://www.frbdiscountwindow.org/Pages/General-Information/Primary-and-Secondary-Lending-Programs) (PCF) housed in the Fed’s [Discount Window](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/d/discountwindow.asp) (DW). The DW functions as the U.S. central bank’s “lender of last resort” (LoLR) system, with the PCF acting as its primary rescue mechanism. This acts alongside the [SRF](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa2cf70b2-965a-48b4-b018-492b8eee4f1d_5574x3598.png) (Standing Repo Facility), the “dealer of last resort”, which injects emergency cash into [repo markets](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) — where some players don’t have Discount Window access.

The BTFP exists as a near replica of the PCF. The former permits banks to borrow cash equal to the full value of securities it pledges with no deductions, providing that these assets existed on the bank’s books before March 13th, 2023. Meanwhile, the PCF allows pledging of any Fed-approved security purchased at any time, though banks only receive funding equal to the security’s market value, minus an additional percentage — known as a [haircut](https://www.frbdiscountwindow.org/Pages/Collateral/collateral_valuation) or [margin](https://www.frbdiscountwindow.org/Pages/Collateral/collateral_valuation) — to protect the central bank from losses in the security’s value during the loan period. For potential losses incurred on BTFP loans, the Fed has received $25 billion in credit protection from the U.S. Treasury. The only other major distinction is the PCF extends loans for a maximum of 90 days, unlike the BTFP’s lengthy 1-year term limit. These differences and similarities have made the Fed recognize how futile the BTFP has become. But more significantly, it’s alerted them to weaknesses in their primary rescue mechanism. During the ideal scenario for which it was conceived, the Discount Window proved ineffective.

Thus, ever since the banking panic and subsequent recovery, the [global regulatory establishment](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression) has been [devising](https://group30.org/images/uploads/publications/G30_Lessons-23-Crisis_RPT_Final.pdf) significant changes to central banks’ primary rescue mechanisms. And since the Fed is the sole dollar provider of last resort, changes to its Discount Window will be most critical and likely replicated worldwide.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eVBm!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F532e0c15-a6b0-40f1-91be-51c4e537d3fe_7041x5088.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!eVBm!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F532e0c15-a6b0-40f1-91be-51c4e537d3fe_7041x5088.png)

same graphic as above, added for convenience

The major weakness exposed by the SVB banking panic — in monetary leaders’ eyes, was the lack of urgency from the Fed and its member banks to achieve “operational readiness,” the ability to access instant central bank liquidity in a crisis. The mismanagement from SVB executives was demonstrated further by a Fed [report](https://www.federalreserve.gov/publications/files/svb-review-20230428.pdf), which discovered that the bank wasn’t ready to receive emergency funding. Other [reports](https://www.reuters.com/business/finance/many-small-us-banks-not-ready-borrow-fed-an-emergency-2023-08-02/#:~:text=The%20lightning%2Dfast%20collapse%20of,among%20the%20nation's%20smallest%20banks.) indicated that even the majority of smaller banks, the most vulnerable to runs, have yet to complete the steps required to obtain instant Fed credit. Only most major banks have carried out adequate measures.

Consequently, monetary leaders are considering sweeping changes. Yet, increasing the maximum loan period, lowering borrowing costs in certain scenarios, and recommending “operational readiness” will be regarded as small modifications. The major objective is to liberate access to the Fed’s PCF during crises by reducing the longstanding stigma associated with using the facility. To achieve this, some leaders want most banks to pledge enough assets in advance at the Fed to cover all “runnable” deposits, which will prompt a sea of new regulatory adaptations. Ultimately, however, this will lead to more oversight from the Fed. The U.S. central bank’s power and control, as [anticipated](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-federal-reserves-gambit), is only set to expand.

_**Coming soon: The Repo Market Rewire™…**_
