# The Central Bank "Pledgening" - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-central-bank-pledgening
> Wall Street and the Fed are about to grow more interlinked

---
Over a year after a systemic panic wiped out the riskiest banks on the planet, the Federal Reserve has completed the phase-out of its [BTFP](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-rescue-mechanism) (Bank Term Funding Program) facility, seemingly without upset. The mini-banking crisis averted by its creation has exposed the regulatory establishment's decadeslong effort to thwart systemic risk as defective. But rather than accepting defeat, monetary leaders are about to double down on averting bank runs, not just in the U.S. but globally. The Central Bank “Pledgening” is about to begin.

The epic run on Silicon Valley Bank (SVB) in March 2023 prompted the downfall of the world’s most feeble banking corporations, from the notorious Credit Suisse to the SVB franchise itself. Yet a swift rescue of the U.S. regional — and ultimately global — banking system has diverted attention away from those who induced the SVB calamity. Only upon the fintech giant’s destruction have monetary officials turned instead to focus on the now-apparent defects in the [Discount Window](https://www.federalreserve.gov/regreform/discount-window.htm): the U.S. central bank’s official mechanism for preventing systemic crises. Following months of planning and indecision, Fed officials will soon unveil a revamped version of their “primary” defensive weapon.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BKG4!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2d4cdac6-c29a-400e-b9a1-ea4bf9a7aadc_7735x7295.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!BKG4!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2d4cdac6-c29a-400e-b9a1-ea4bf9a7aadc_7735x7295.png)

Acting as an onshore ceiling within the Fed’s global dollar rates [hierarchy](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-global-dollar-rates?r=njk1&utm_campaign=post&utm_medium=web), the Discount Window has enabled the U.S. central bank to deliver endless liquidity to America’s banking colossus, all while deterring its benchmark interest rate, Federal Funds ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)), i.e. the interbank market rate to borrow reserves, from breaching officials’ target boundaries. Though imperfect, the Discount Window has averted most interbank squeezes by supplying reserves via numerous facilities, each with differing terms of entry. The most prominent and accessible by far, however, remains the Primary Credit Facility, or PCF for short. Serving as the Fed’s official rescue mechanism, the PCF provides almost all Discount Window loans to ostensibly sound banks desperate for reserves, which they use to try to stay in business during fierce outflows. The PCF relieves the pressure of heavy withdrawals, where too many depositors transfer their cash to other banks, usually in times of turmoil. Instead of “firesaling” assets (selling securities at a loss) to raise the reserves needed to process outflows, solvent yet illiquid banks can pledge virtually any triple-A rated asset to the U.S. central bank — from Treasuries to foreign currency bonds to CDOs — in exchange for emergency dollars.

Central bank access, though, comes at a price. To discourage overuse and stimulate private sector activity, the Fed charges borrowers a penalty rate for tapping the PCF, dubbed the “discount rate”. Its name originates from how the PCF acts as the Discount Window’s major “lender of last resort” facility, not from its cost of borrowing, which the Fed sets at a premium to private rates — not at a discount as its name suggests. Fixing the discount rate significantly above the Fed Funds rate should prompt banks to tap the Fed’s emergency facility only if a liquidity squeeze emerges. As their cost to borrow in the Fed Funds market surpasses the PCF, Fed liquidity becomes the cheapest alternative until interbank rates fall back into balance. Subsequently, the Fed setting the discount rate to equal the upper bound of its target range enforces a maximum rate for well-capitalized banks to obtain reserves. At least, in normal times.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oqEu!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd0dc3725-2c43-456a-b3ac-5f53e3a303e6_6126x5590.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oqEu!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd0dc3725-2c43-456a-b3ac-5f53e3a303e6_6126x5590.png)

In times of turmoil, however, as numerous episodes have demonstrated, banks aren’t always willing to borrow from the Fed’s official lender-of-last-resort mechanism. Facing the stigma of appearing troubled to their counterparties, banks have retreated from tapping the PCF even when it’s cheaper, causing rates to blow through the Fed’s acceptable upper limits. Crises such as the GFC (Great Financial Crisis of 2008) and the 2019 “[repocalypse](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-global-dollar-endgame)” instead revealed that increased intervention was the true lender of last resort (and the true ceiling on interbank rates), with the Fed inventing a myriad of facilities just to convince banks to access emergency funding. The recent creation (and following expiration) of the Fed’s BTFP was the latest reminder that central bank alchemy has remained the silent but genuine dollar backstop. Even so, the Discount Window’s PCF has preserved its handle as the Fed’s official lender of last resort (LoLR) mechanism.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4mYq!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fec4ebf87-7409-478f-8a49-da3496c7a7e3_6907x5589.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4mYq!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fec4ebf87-7409-478f-8a49-da3496c7a7e3_6907x5589.png)

before and after the Fed’s BTFP backstop

Sixteen years after the GFC, the financial system has transitioned to a “secured standard,” where banks operate as utilities and shadow banks absorb most of the risk, knowing monetary authorities likely have their back in a crisis. The SVB banking panic has merely ushered in the next iteration of the secured standard, with fortifying central bank mechanisms being one of many new regulatory objectives. In the past few months, monetary leaders have hinted at their proposed modifications to the Discount Window, which ironically have been designed to prevent increased intervention. Yet this will also spur more interlinkage between the Fed and Wall Street via a “great pledge” of collateral. What does this mean in practice? Let’s unveil the mechanics.

Six months ago, in January 2024, a group of central bank thought leaders, known as the [Group of 30](https://www.group30.org/), released their [vision](https://group30.org/images/uploads/publications/G30_Lessons-23-Crisis_RPT_Final.pdf) for the future of the Discount Window and other LoLR (lender of last resort) mechanisms globally. Numerous proposals were presented, with the most prominent being an effort to destroy the longstanding stigma around using the Fed’s official rescue mechanism — one that’s persisted for decades in corporate banking culture — by encouraging public officials to normalize its usage. The report included other ways to thwart systemic bank runs outside of altering the Discount Window, like removing the ability for banks to offset enormous unrealized losses on their securities holdings when complying with regulations plus increasing deposit insurance provided by the [FDIC](https://www.fdic.gov/) (Federal Deposit Insurance Corporation). What’s more, the first systemic run in the social media era even sparked the concept of opening the Discount Window 24/7. Still, amidst many novel proposals, the most significant centered around the financial plumbing.

In what _Conks_ calls the Central Bank Pledgening[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-central-bank-pledgening#footnote-1-145222233), monetary leaders proposed that banks hold sufficient liquidity to fulfill most outflows of “flighty” liabilities. This involves banks hoarding cash (i.e. bank reserves) and pledging a chunk of their assets to the Fed at the Discount Window, enough to cover a specified share of liabilities likely to be pulled at short notice. These so-called runnable liabilities — or “[runnables](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econresdata/notes/feds-notes/2015/the-runnables-20150903.html)” — consist mostly of uninsured deposits exceeding the $250k FDIC-insured limit, which large depositors could withdraw from vulnerable banks within a few clicks. Known in Fed-speak as “prepositioning,” most banks would need to initiate and maintain a substantial pledge of assets to the Fed’s official defense mechanism, securing the option to tap central bank liquidity whenever a squeeze emerges. In theory, this assures regulators and, more importantly, depositors that banks have sufficient arsenals to handle severe outflows — ironically, the same outflows that prepositioning collateral aims to discourage.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3fXi!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff2e5d1d0-b700-49b8-98df-75c66edddd71_4870x3486.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3fXi!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Ff2e5d1d0-b700-49b8-98df-75c66edddd71_4870x3486.png)

the central bank pledgening visualized

Today, the Central Bank Pledgening has already been endorsed by Fed officials, who will shortly announce a string of new regulatory modifications. Banks have caught wind of an impending Discount Window liberation, with central bankers calling on public officials to help “destigmatize” the facility. Meanwhile, sources have revealed that the Fed’s initial “runnable ratio” — the portion of runnables needing to be covered by reserves and pledged collateral — should land around 40%, prompting banks and other depository institutions to preposition billions in extra collateral at the Discount Window.

Though, with each banking mishap, this ratio will likely increase. Many have already called for 100% backing of runnables, the effects of which have yet to be fully understood. Banks operating as major market makers, already constrained by requirements to hoard reserves and other high-quality liquid assets (HQLA), may be unable to make markets as efficiently — causing officials to backpedal or adjust other regulations to compensate, including [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!u8YA!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8db19da4-44a8-42b7-9728-60cfc098f179_5958x5674.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!u8YA!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8db19da4-44a8-42b7-9728-60cfc098f179_5958x5674.png)

Whatever level the Fed chooses, the Central Bank Pledgening will reinforce the core doctrine of the [secured standard](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-rise-of-the-secured-standard): transferring undesirable private sector risk to the sovereign level, granting officials more power and control over money and markets. To comply with a cluster of regulations, most financial entities have been forced to hoard U.S. sovereign liabilities, mainly Treasuries and reserve balances held at the Fed. These allocations will continue to grow, as will the mechanisms that increase the Fed’s authority over global finance. Alongside its active “[volatility suppressor](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression)”, where one Fed announcement can stabilize key securities markets, the U.S. central bank has assembled a passive counterpart. Just like the last decade or so, recent regulatory changes — namely [the Basel III Endgame](https://www.pwc.com/us/en/industries/financial-services/library/basel-iii-endgame.html) and the coming [money market fund reforms](https://www.sec.gov/files/33-11211-fact-sheet.pdf) — create a structural demand for sovereign assets (liabilities), which monetary leaders can both easily supply and rescue.

The Central Bank Pledgening only boosts this structural demand further. Paired with constraints such as Basel III’s LCR — where banks must hold enough liquidity to handle 30 days of outflows, the need to satisfy the Fed’s new runnable ratio will help reinforce demand for reserves and U.S. government-related securities. The idea that the Fed’s balance sheet normalizes and debt issuance decreases will become an even more distant memory. Excess is the new normal, and the secured standard demands it.

Once the looming upgrades to the Discount Window are phased in, the financial system will ultimately achieve more resilience. Yet economic gravity always finds a way to create and exploit new loopholes. Bank runs can’t be eliminated, a reality that even top Fed officials acknowledge. The next systemic panic will prompt another unique, unfamiliar Fed response and subsequent intervention, with the true lender of the last resort mechanism, central bank alchemy, reemerging from the shadows once again.

_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Global Put: Part I - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i
> the U.S. central bank's global dollar backstop and the end of QT

---
The U.S. central bank is set to [strengthen](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-central-bank-pledgening) its primary defense mechanism, the Discount Window, deterring systemic bank runs while manufacturing greater demand for reserves and U.S. sovereign debt. This will be one of multiple catalysts forcing dollar market makers to hoard liquidity, decreasing their readiness to step in as _private_ lenders of last resort. Consequently, the Fed’s swap line network — its global defense mechanism — will evolve into a casual source of dollars and less of a backstop, the opposite of what the Fed intends. The U.S. central bank will thus have another motive to end its latest round of QT[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-1-145918491) and boost liquidity earlier than expected. Frictions at the top of the dollar rates hierarchy are growing.

Amidst the peak of the subprime meltdown in 2008, a disconnect in the opaquest realm of money markets compelled Fed officials to enforce a truly global dollar backstop. The infamous Lehman Brothers collapse on Sept 15th caused the spread between onshore dollar rates, 3-month OIS[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-2-145918491), and offshore dollar rates, 3-month LIBOR[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-3-145918491), to widen significantly[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-4-145918491). With offshore rates spiking, the fantasy that a global dollar standard was already in effect had been exposed. In response, officials fortified the bridge between offshore and onshore dollar markets by engaging in unprecedented levels of intervention, bailing out the entire money market fund (MMF) complex, cutting rates sharply, and doubling the maximum dollars available to be tapped via swap lines — enacted previously in late 2007 to avert rising turmoil. Nevertheless, the LIBOR-OIS spread kept widening. An enhanced ceiling on global dollar rates was incoming[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-5-145918491).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!XVIH!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0f6b89c-8c6d-4847-9863-1af85f47b76d_6126x5590.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!XVIH!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0f6b89c-8c6d-4847-9863-1af85f47b76d_6126x5590.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

The Fed had two choices to set a tougher ceiling on global dollar rates: enhance its Discount Window or swap line network. The [Discount Window](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-central-bank-pledgening) allowed foreign banks to pledge collateral at their New York branches, receive emergency dollars from the Fed, extend those dollars to HQ (headquarters), and then lend to frantic offshore borrowers. Swap lines, meanwhile, added foreign central banks into the mix. Acting as brokers, foreign central banks held dollar auctions in their home country, where domestic banks seeking dollars bid for liquidity by pledging sovereign bonds[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-6-145918491) as collateral plus a [haircut](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/h/haircut.asp), shielding the central bank from potential losses. The foreign central bank then transferred dollar reserves, credited to its master (reserve) account[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-7-145918491) by the Fed, to the foreign banks’ master account. The foreign bank had now acquired access to dollars and could issue dollar loans to customers at its offshore branches. The “cross-border currency repo” (CBCR) was born.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QmAt!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0aa43b2-7070-4711-920c-1c795343f6a2_6747x5053.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QmAt!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0aa43b2-7070-4711-920c-1c795343f6a2_6747x5053.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

While a global discount window failed to provide dollars to smaller, less connected foreign banks — and their $6 trillion in liabilities, a global swap network could reach every corner of the offshore dollar system and repair the LIBOR-OIS breakage. On October 13th, 2008, the Fed announced unlimited swap lines with every major Western central bank, then the BoJ (Bank of Japan) the following day, bringing the spread back into balance. Limitless “cross-border currency repos” was the fix that would grow to become the new global dollar backstop. Yet, it had not reached its full potency. More than a decade later, a global pandemic would allow monetary leaders to usher in a more powerful weapon.

By the time COVID arrived in 2020, the financial system had transformed. Banks were now flush with reserves due to the Fed’s [ample reserves](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/implementing-monetary-policy-in-an-ample-reserves-regime-the-basics-note-1-of-3-20200701.html) regime. The “Big Three” — Basel III, Dodd-Frank, and SEC money market reform — had forced banks to hoard even more U.S. sovereign liabilities and avoid excessive risk-taking. As the COVID market panic intensified, the 3-month LIBOR-OIS spread again detached. This time, unlike in 2008, a more reactive Fed broadened access to its swap lines and offered considerable quantities in days, not months. But again, the spread kept widening. The U.S. central bank lowered the penalty rate to obtain dollars through swap lines from OIS + 50bps to OIS + 25bps, offered longer-term swaps, and extended lines to nine additional central banks globally.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oHpT!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc80184d8-81ba-4db1-adc1-db9e67557c60_1919x1242.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!oHpT!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fc80184d8-81ba-4db1-adc1-db9e67557c60_1919x1242.png)

Source: Atlanta Fed

Even so, the spread between onshore and offshore dollar rates peaked only after the Fed vowed to provide daily swap line liquidity. Using the GFC (Great Financial Crisis) textbook, the Fed offered unlimited dollar swaps but at much lower rates and to a broader audience, providing cross-border currency repos to all foreign banks needing dollars.

After foreign central banks announced local dollar auctions via major trading platforms — like Refinitiv and Bloomberg — domestic banks would submit bids and post sovereign bonds as collateral to their foreign central bank’s designated repo system. Swiss bank branches, for instance, transferred securities on the [SIX](https://www.six-group.com/en/products-services/securities-services/securities-finance/repo.html) repo platform to the SNB (Swiss National Bank). This collateral, plus a small margin payment posted by domestic banks, protected foreign central banks from harm if those banks defaulted. The first “leg” of the cross-border currency repo was completed.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QmAt!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0aa43b2-7070-4711-920c-1c795343f6a2_6747x5053.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!QmAt!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe0aa43b2-7070-4711-920c-1c795343f6a2_6747x5053.png)

same graphic as above, added here for convenience

In the second (and final) leg, central banks worked together via voice or electronic communication to channel dollars to offshore banks. By entering into “central bank reserve swaps”, the Fed and the foreign central bank expanded their balance sheets, creating reserves, and then credited each other’s reserve accounts at the opposite central bank, boosting global liquidity. Straight after, the foreign central bank instructed the Fed — via SWIFT messages — to transfer newly minted reserves to the New York branch of foreign banks, which had bid for dollars via their domestic headquarters. This extensive procedure evolved to not only shield central banks from losses but allow banks to obtain dollars lawfully under the post-2008 regulatory order.

With new liquidity regulations, namely the LCR[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-8-145918491) (Liquidity Coverage Ratio) now binding, internationally active banks have had to hold enough high-quality liquid assets (dubbed “HQLA” by Basel III architects) not on a domestic level but on a globally consolidated basis. Offshore dollar deposits booked at a branch in one country must be backed by liquid dollar assets held at a U.S. affiliate. That means obtaining reserves (Fed Funds), the premier type of HQLA, at their New York branches, which have access to dollar reserves issued within the Federal Reserve System.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YSxs!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa85efe0e-8faa-491a-b4f7-e8b9ebd022f9_1268x1332.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YSxs!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa85efe0e-8faa-491a-b4f7-e8b9ebd022f9_1268x1332.png)

make a dollar loan at an offshore branch (C) and back it by borrowing dollar reserves at your U.S. branch (D) | Source: BIS

Once foreign banks received sought-after reserves, the COVID dollar squeeze was resolved. Using liquidity obtained from swap lines, foreign banks could now make dollar loans to customers and settle associated transactions with other banks. The Fed’s new and improved backstop saved the day.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!k82-!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F892660e7-b2e1-428a-9469-7c918e8049e6_872x563.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!k82-!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F892660e7-b2e1-428a-9469-7c918e8049e6_872x563.png)

latest central bank FX swap data | Source: New York Fed

Fast forward to late 2024, and contrary to popular belief, the Fed’s global swap line network is still in operation. It doesn’t need “turning on”. Daily auctions remain in effect between the Fed and foreign central banks, mostly the ECB, Bank of England, and BoJ, who broadcast dollar liquidity to entities in their jurisdictions. Once reserves are received from their central banks, the recipient foreign banks can even issue dollar loans cross-border to borrowers in other locations. A large bank in Germany, for instance, can obtain dollars to provide FX swap liquidity to a corporate customer in Sweden desperate to hedge their dollar exposure. Following the GFC, these “emergency” central bank FX swaps have persisted.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!FwaL!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F734b7b4a-7ed9-4a02-b411-0374396d0406_4523x4853.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!FwaL!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F734b7b4a-7ed9-4a02-b411-0374396d0406_4523x4853.png)

the latest money market stress monitor

Not only that, but spikes in swap line volume now appear on period-ends, like during year-end in 2023. In such instances, the cost to make markets has grown so large that banks have pulled back from serving as private “FX swappers of last resort”, forcing dollar borrowers to turn to the Fed[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-9-145918491) for assistance. Post-GFC rules have ended the concept of limitless leverage, where large financial players could quickly expand their balance sheets to exploit any minor dislocation. Before the subprime turmoil erupted, rates in different dollar funding markets thus traded closely. Afterward, the onslaught of capital and liquidity regulations pulled these dollar rates apart to form a distinct hierarchy _(as shown below)_.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftn6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb85c7ec5-69a3-4abf-8bbf-5a6210fc0765_6456x3634.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftn6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb85c7ec5-69a3-4abf-8bbf-5a6210fc0765_6456x3634.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

The secured lending markets (repos) and unsecured lending markets (i.e. commercial paper and Eurodollars) lie at the bottom. Meanwhile, FX swaps — which require higher compensation for swapping U.S. dollars for foreign currency — sit at the top. If a foreign bank has limited access to lower layers, it pursues dollars at the upper layers of the hierarchy via FX swaps. Hierarchies have even formed within hierarchies. In the FX swap market, dealers form the base by connecting most dollar lenders and borrowers seeking FX swap liquidity, matching investors hedging currency exposures and speculators betting on FX derivatives[10](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-10-145918491). At the upper layers, however, the private dollar lenders of last resort settle any remaining imbalances at a premium rate. Foreign banks are the first to take over, with superior access to unsecured dollar funding markets. U.S. banks then act as the “last line of defense” before Fed swap lines are tapped, using government-sponsored funding from Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs).

The Fed steps in if the large U.S. banking giants fail to deliver. Yet, there’s a hidden arbitrary limit to casual, non-urgent swap line usage. Ironically, monetary leaders don’t want to intervene “too much” and become regular market makers unless it’s necessary to keep rates within boundaries. Keeping reserves abundant is a straightforward fix.

Coincidentally, conflicts have emerged at the top of the dollar rates hierarchy, where the ultimate decision to tap central bank swaps is made. The pipes are becoming constricted, and it’s not just the Fed’s QT (quantitative tightening) impeding private dollar market makers.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!WJRX!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F314ca053-7bcb-490c-8919-c72caf49462a_5721x3563.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!WJRX!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F314ca053-7bcb-490c-8919-c72caf49462a_5721x3563.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

Following the events of the banking panic in 2023, FHLBs are likely set to lend to fewer banks that bridge dollar imbalances before the Fed has to intervene. Fewer private lenders of last resort[11](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i#footnote-11-145918491) equals more swap line activity. What’s more, the FHFA — the FHLB’s regulator — may introduce these measures right when Fed officials consider ending QT, possibly by early 2025. The U.S. central bank has already slowed the pace of its balance sheet reduction, with market participants moving down the risk curve. Ending QT will provide yet another “plumbing put”, with the overuse of swap line liquidity being an unexpected motive.


_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button below the footnotes to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Global Put: Part II - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii
> a multitude of catalysts could bring an end to QT

---
_— click [here](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i) for The Fed’s Global Put: Part I_

A cluster of rules forcing banking giants to stockpile liquidity, paired with the Fed’s QT[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-1-148913155) (quantitative tightening), has [restricted](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-i) the private lenders of last resort from policing the top of the dollar rates [hierarchy](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-global-dollar-rates). On its own, this may not force the Fed to cease its most extensive balance sheet reduction (i.e. taper) on record. Yet, a medley of incoming catalysts could prompt reserves to once again fall to unstable levels, which central bank models may fail to anticipate. With a series of liquidity blindspots ahead, the Fed will soon exercise[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-2-148913155) its next “plumbing put.”

Halfway through 2017, alongside the undead [Fed Funds](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-death-of-the-interbank-market) market, a parallel interbank ecosystem began to emerge. Fed officials had forced global systemically important banks, known as [G-SIBs](https://www.fsb.org/2023/11/2023-list-of-global-systemically-important-banks-g-sibs/)[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-3-148913155), and other large systemic players to protect the banking system from their own demise. These financial behemoths began submitting what the Fed called “living wills,” [resolution plans](https://www.federalreserve.gov/supervisionreg/resolution-plans.htm) to allow officials to quietly wind down failed banks’ operations while avoiding another Great Financial Crisis (GFC). A post-crisis attempt by Fed architects to eradicate “too big to fail” megabanks had been initiated.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!khQZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd702d685-c602-47d9-86b5-eb2da67e51a0_5721x3563.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!khQZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd702d685-c602-47d9-86b5-eb2da67e51a0_5721x3563.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

Subsequently, out of numerous constraints that followed, the Fed ordered the largest Wall Street banks to maintain not only adequate end-of-day liquidity — to satisfy Basel III — but also “resolution liquidity,” a sufficient _intraday_ cushion, to fulfill new regulations[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-4-148913155). Every one of a bank’s entities had to maintain an around-the-clock buffer of high-quality liquid assets (HQLA)[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-5-148913155). Drop below at any time and face the wrath of the regulator. By mid-2017, the U.S. central bank’s rules had grown even more ruthless, so much so it was time for Basel III to step aside as the primary constraint on banking giants. With the Fed’s living wills acting as extra shackles, a market for intraday megabank liquidity was set to emerge.

As it happened, the perfect ecosystem already existed but had yet to be utilized. It wasn’t the [Fed Funds](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-death-of-the-interbank-market) market, which remained almost deserted post-2008 but was far from extinct. Unable to earn interest ([IORB](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/reserve-balances.htm)) on their reserve balances, [Federal Home Loan Banks](https://fhlbanks.com/) (FHLBs) lent billions to foreign banks[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-6-148913155) engaging in a popular arbitrage trade. Unlike U.S banks, the New York branches of foreign banks paid no FDIC deposit insurance fees, allowing them to earn a profitable spread from borrowing Fed Funds overnight (o/n FF) and parking these in their reserves accounts — earning IORB. Foreign banks, plus the largest regional banks, also discovered they could borrow Fed Funds to enhance their regulatory metrics (e.g. Basel III’s LCR) on key reporting dates. Moreover, whenever rates in other money markets spiked sharply above IORB, a rare window opened for large banks and custodian banks[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-7-148913155) to borrow Fed Funds and deploy them into money markets paying higher rates — like repos and FX swaps. Small domestic banks, without a gigantic stock of reserves like G-SIBs, also regularly entered the Fed Funds market to acquire interbank liquidity.

Large U.S. banks, however, had no reason to enter the Fed Funds market other than to meet sudden regulatory shortfalls. Even so, due to a few timing technicalities, o/n FF trades did not satisfy G-SIB’s new intraday liquidity requirements. Large banks had to find an alternative, and the solution came from the most dominant interbank participant.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2Y79!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8ee2249a-98be-4abd-b3f5-2dba4d52253a_3123x2188.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2Y79!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F8ee2249a-98be-4abd-b3f5-2dba4d52253a_3123x2188.png)

Making up ~97% of all Fed Funds lending, the FHLBs began supplying reserves to large banks desperate to comply with resolution liquidity, via the only instrument with such a capability: interest-bearing deposit accounts, known as “IBDAs”[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-8-148913155). Tapping reserves via the regular unsecured interbank market was not only costly for large banks but also failed to satisfy round-the-clock liquidity requirements. Overnight Fed Funds trading involved a slim window in which lenders (FHLBs) returned funds to borrowers (banks) for a few hours in the early morning before renewing trades and wiring funds back between reserve accounts. IBDAs, though, functioned as deposit accounts, not renewed overnight loans, and provided sufficient coverage to avoid intraday shortfalls[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#footnote-9-148913155). The price for megabanks to preserve end-of-day and intraday liquidity via a single mechanism was a higher rate (5-10bps above the Fed’s target range!) but a price worth paying. Non-compliance was even more costly.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hFPN!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4815ddcd-6705-483a-85da-6e7f6e017590_6601x5734.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hFPN!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F4815ddcd-6705-483a-85da-6e7f6e017590_6601x5734.png)

top left: IBDA rates trading at a hefty premium to Fed Funds

By the back half of 2018, the IBDA market had grown from near-zero to almost $15 billion in volume, nearly a quarter of Fed Funds, even with IBDA rates climbing above the Fed’s offerings at its [Discount Window](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-central-bank-pledgening). With increased demand from G-SIBs, prompted by the Fed’s prior balance sheet taper sucking reserves from the system, the market for intraday megabank liquidity prospered. By early 2020, the COVID-19 market panic emerged, and the U.S. central bank’s massive QE injections flushed bank balance sheets with reserves. Demand for IBDAs collapsed accordingly, with volumes plummeting to just $5 billion. A year after the panic subsided, however, the Fed had initiated its most epic balance reduction on record, dubbed QT2.

Up to today, this taper remains in effect. The Fed’s most lengthy balance sheet runoff continues to reduce reserves by ~$60 billion monthly — down from $95 billion monthly since June this year. G-SIBs’ intraday liquidity needs have thus become impossible to meet without regularly tapping reserves via IBDAs. Volumes in this intraday liquidity market have consequently soared to all-time highs, even as reserves appear plentiful and the Fed shows zero willingness to cease decimating the supply of reserve balances. Fed Chair Powell, when [grilled](https://www.federalreserve.gov/mediacenter/files/FOMCpresconf20240918.pdf) at the latest September FOMC meeting, expected interbank liquidity to remain ample “for some time” while revealing that the Fed considered QT no longer tightening but a “policy normalization tool” when paired with rate cuts.

Nevertheless, numerous potential blindspots could emerge, ending QT by early to mid-2025. Presently, reserve balances of key market makers continue to dwindle, with the Fed’s balance sheet runoff persisting. Once an offsetting force, the Fed’s RRP is now close to zero. [Sponsored repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-sponsored-repo?utm_source=publication-search) has boosted the number of non-Fed counterparties with which to trade, while hedge fund demand has pushed repo rates high enough to attract more cash lenders out of the Fed’s facility. Banks, now unable to resist, have started to lend more of their reserves into repos, draining liquidity from other corners of the banking system. The upper layers of the [repo hierarchy](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-reverse-repo-endgame) have been triggered.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!95kG!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa72f777b-2fce-4ee0-8330-60047ab2c112_8273x7836.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!95kG!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa72f777b-2fce-4ee0-8330-60047ab2c112_8273x7836.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

Meanwhile, following the SVB calamity, the FHFA — the FHLB’s regulator — will soon begin to levy new [proposed](https://www.fhfa.gov/sites/default/files/2024-01/FHLBank-System-at-100-Report.pdf) measures. IBDAs are set to flourish with greater regulatory support, yet the FHFA has vowed to reduce access to FHLB liquidity via Fed Funds, employing stricter ratings and lending limits. A lower supply from a major Fed Funds lender (not via IBDAs) means you narrow the chances of large players making a market — the same large players already experiencing steady or reducing reserve balances. If large U.S. banks can’t arbitrage via FHLBs, they must use “spare reserves”, which are in ever shorter supply. And when your regular private lender of last resort fails to deliver, you must trade with your irregular public lender of last resort. Swap line activity is thus set to expand, and not just on stormy [quarter-ends](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-great-flattening#:~:text=Yet%20another%20%E2%80%9Cquarter%2Dend%E2%80%9D%3A%20the%20last%20day%20of%20each%20quarter%20on%20the%20financial%20calendar%20and%20the%20most%20likely%20to%20encounter%20volatility). Minus these episodes, a billion dollars in central bank FX swaps is historically a sign of tight interbank conditions.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftn6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb85c7ec5-69a3-4abf-8bbf-5a6210fc0765_6456x3634.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ftn6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb85c7ec5-69a3-4abf-8bbf-5a6210fc0765_6456x3634.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktops)

It will likely take more than that, however, to unsettle this Fed. In early 2025, another debt limit drama will have developed, bringing the government’s bank account, the [TGA](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/WTREGEN) (Treasury General Account), back into focus. Counterintuitively, a TGA drain, once associated with improved liquidity via reserves re-entering the banking system, increases the risk that the Fed “over drains”. The quantity of t-bills issued by the Treasury is set to diminish in early 2025, lowering yields and making other money market investments, such as the Fed’s RRP, more attractive. Yet, only temporarily. A liquidity mirage will form, where a temporary TGA drain and higher RRP balance make reserves appear more ample than in reality. When the debt limit drama ends, these flows will reverse, impeding the Fed’s ability to gauge whether or not reserve levels remain ample or scarce. Halting QT, of course, around the next debt limit conflict is the straightforward solution.

Even so, despite all this, the Fed likely won’t budge until the ultimate signal of reserve scarcity appears: a rising Fed Funds rate ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)). Once that starts grinding higher toward IORB, the U.S. central bank will quickly end its latest balance sheet taper without hesitation. With a colossal Fed balance sheet being the new status quo, the death of QT will eventually arrive. But only when the undead Fed Funds market comes back to life.

_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button below the footnotes to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Relief Valve: Part I - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i
> how officials plan to boost Treasury market liquidity

---
The U.S. Treasury market’s plumbing is about to receive another greasing. In an attempt to boost liquidity and suppress volatility, the latest band of monetary leaders has vowed to lubricate the pipes of America’s sovereign debt market. An enormous debt load and elevated deficits have prompted U.S. central bank and Treasury officials to grow wary of market dysfunction. Treasury Sec. Bessent and POTUS have publicly [targeted](https://www.reuters.com/markets/us/us-bond-market-lukewarm-bessents-10-year-yield-pledge-2025-02-06/#:~:text=Bessent%20said%20on%20Wednesday%20that,as%20energy%20prices%20will%20drop.) a lower 10-year yield[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-1-156939084) while [delaying](https://home.treasury.gov/policy-issues/financing-the-government/quarterly-refunding/most-recent-quarterly-refunding-documents) an increase in longer-term Treasury issuance, sovereign debt that carries increased duration risk[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-2-156939084). Leaders at the Fed, meanwhile, have performed a “regulatory 180”, declaring their intention to reduce major constraints on dominant UST (i.e. U.S. Treasury) market makers[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-3-156939084). The Fed’s “[relief valve](https://www.sciencedirect.com/topics/engineering/relief-valve#:~:text=Relief%20valves%20are%20used%20in,vapor%2C%20and%2For%20liquid.)” could thus finally be re-opened.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1xel!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F055fc987-f916-4b1e-804a-d8fb96826a0d_6514x6026.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!1xel!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F055fc987-f916-4b1e-804a-d8fb96826a0d_6514x6026.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

Ever since the GFC (Great Financial Crisis) concluded, the [global regulatory complex](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-coming-volatility-suppression?utm_source=publication-search#:~:text=reached%20escape%20velocity.-,The%20Global%20Regulatory%20Complex,-%E2%84%A2%20achieved%20its%20most) has imposed increasingly fierce shackles on the major banking giants — all large bank holding companies ([BHCs](https://www.fedpartnership.gov/bank-life-cycle/grow-shareholder-value/bank-holding-companies)) that operate vast [primary dealer](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/primarydealers) businesses. [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm) and [The Dodd-Frank Act](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act) have transformed these huge “dealer banks”[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-4-156939084) from risk-takers into boring utilities, hampered by relentless capital (and [liquidity](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-global-put-part-ii#:~:text=Subsequently%2C%20out%20of,.)) requirements. To prevent a replay of the GFC, regulators have forced big banks to hold enough capital to offset both the riskiness and size of their balance sheets. Bank risk-taking has been capped by Basel III’s RWA (risk-weighted assets) standard, which mandates banks to maintain “[capital buffers](https://www.jpmorganchase.com/content/dam/jpmc/jpmorgan-chase-and-co/investor-relations/documents/annualreport-2023.pdf#page=135)”[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-5-156939084) above and beyond the riskiness of their exposures. Even so, some “riskless” assets have always turned hazardous eventually[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-6-156939084), prompting monetary architects to invent the [SLR](https://www.risk.net/definition/supplementary-leverage-ratio-slr#:~:text=are%20looking%20for.-,Supplementary%20leverage%20ratio%20(SLR),US%20banks%20must%20hold%203%25.): the supplementary leverage ratio. Alongside risk-based ratios[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-7-156939084), the SLR acts as a “non-risk weighted” constraint, forcing banks to raise equity whenever they expand their balance sheets, no matter the exposure[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-8-156939084). With even U.S. Treasuries and bank reserves now labelled as dangerous, the SLR[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-9-156939084) has compelled dealer banks to somewhat pull back from providing liquidity in the most systemic regions of global finance.

But with a new POTUS in power, a deregulatory wind is blowing. A series of out-of-the-blue statements by top central bankers suggests the Fed will exempt (i.e. remove) USTs and other exposures from the SLR. Obscure markets that capture constraints on key Treasury market makers[10](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-10-156939084) have thus started pricing in an unwind of the harshest plumbing climate on record, deepened by a vast supply of Treasuries and heightened deficits[11](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-11-156939084). Nevertheless, for numerous political and plumbing reasons, the Fed may fail to deliver a great easing of dealer constraints, an outcome being priced out of — not into — markets. A regulatory “rug pull’ is looming.

Over the past few months, Trump’s leverage over the Fed has enabled deregulatory champions to acquire key positions of influence. Facing presidential pressure, the [Fed’s Vice Chair (VC) for Supervision](https://www.federalreserve.gov/aboutthefed/bios/board/barr.htm), Michael Barr (the architect of the Basel III Endgame[12](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-12-156939084) and a pro-regulation advocate) has stepped down while staying on the Board, a savvy sacrifice that legally protects the U.S. central bank’s current leadership from a Trumpian ousting. In Barr’s place, Fed Governor [Bowman](https://www.federalreserve.gov/aboutthefed/bios/board/bowman.htm) or [Waller](https://www.federalreserve.gov/aboutthefed/bios/board/waller.htm) will likely serve as the new VC, two board members with relatively covert Republican roots. But since POTUS has previously endorsed Bowman, who has sworn to eliminate much of the Fed’s red tape, the former state bank commissioner is destined to become the primary “influencer” of U.S. banking policy — more influential than Treasury Sec. Bessent[13](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-13-156939084).

While the prospect of a deregulatory VC grows stronger, markets have digested a feast of comments from officials proposing relaxed constraints on dominant market makers, rules that supposedly prevent dealer banks from intermediating Treasuries[14](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-14-156939084). Just as the February [QRA](https://home.treasury.gov/policy-issues/financing-the-government/quarterly-refunding/most-recent-quarterly-refunding-documents)[15](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-15-156939084) revealed Bessent had delayed more longer-term UST issuance, the Fed’s Bowman [hinted](https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/bowman20250205a.htm) at loosening bank SLRs. Only a few days later, Fed Chair Powell asserted during a [testimony](https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=n64DO1xFG70) that an SLR rework was overdue, vowing to “get it done”. Swap spreads[16](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-16-156939084), which capture dealer constraints, abruptly priced an easing in plumbing conditions. The spread between USTs and swaps, driven by — among other factors — the differing capital cost[17](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-17-156939084) to satisfy the SLR, widened[18](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-18-156939084) sharply, foreseeing a greasing of UST liquidity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!x8o5!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb53ca900-25f0-493a-a321-d2c32cd3d212_4350x3089.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!x8o5!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb53ca900-25f0-493a-a321-d2c32cd3d212_4350x3089.png)

the chart exacerbates the 30y swap spread with a dual y-axis, but 30y swap spreads still moved a lot more than shorter-term spreads

These market moves, plus official statements, have calmed increasing worries of a Treasury plumbing bottleneck. The primary concern[19](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-19-156939084) is that market makers won’t possess adequate balance sheet to absorb an [ever-increasing](https://fiscaldata.treasury.gov/americas-finance-guide/national-debt/) barrage of USTs. So far, though, primary dealers have carried such a cargo without trouble, made possible by (what most would consider) a dysfunctional market structure. Minus an emergency, concentrated trading in [on-the-runs](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/on-the-runtreasuries.asp) and limited trading in [off-the-runs](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/off-the-runtreasuries.asp) has enabled the U.S. empire to expand its debt load without severe illiquidity[20](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-20-156939084). Around four-fifths of [cash market](http://conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-us-treasury-cash)[21](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-21-156939084) volumes [occur](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/wp-content/uploads/sites/2/2018/11/6a01348793456c970c022ad3c318b1200b-500wi.jpg) in the latest issues (i.e. on-the-run securities), which dealers gradually [offload](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-treasury-market#:~:text=The%20most%20common%20transaction%20in%20the%20primary%20market%20involves%20the%20Federal%20Reserve%E2%80%99s%20primary%20dealers%20buying%20most%20of%20the%20auctioned%20securities%20to%20distribute%20among%20a%20wide%20range%20of%20clients%2C%20from%20asset%20managers%20to%20hedge%20funds.) from their inventories into investor portfolios, remaining there until maturity. As these Treasuries turn into older, off-the-run issues, liquidity grows scarcer. Sellers will encounter limited buyers and wider market spreads.

Regardless, as the Treasury continues to “soft intervene” via [buybacks](https://treasurydirect.gov/auctions/announcements-data-results/buy-backs/), liquidity has stayed ample even in the most vulnerable regions. Dealers and their end-customers buy bonds first, and, if required, the sovereign “neutralizes” these assets by buying them back (and replacing them with fresh issues) later. Any regular demand to sell illiquid USTs has been met by the Treasury’s liquidity program with primary dealers, presently in [high demand](https://treasurydirect.gov/auctions/announcements-data-results/buy-backs/) at specific parts of the curve. Any panic selling, meanwhile, has been cured by the [emergency wing](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/openmarket.htm) at the New York Fed.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5l5o!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F77c3ed82-3adc-4810-90c5-bbef24fc0ec5_1200x675.jpeg)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!5l5o!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F77c3ed82-3adc-4810-90c5-bbef24fc0ec5_1200x675.jpeg)

The hysteria around waning liquidity is likely overdone. Nonetheless, an SLR exemption will grease the Treasury market’s plumbing, freeing up space on the balance sheets of key UST market makers: the “Big Six” dealer banks[22](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-22-156939084). These banking behemoths compute their SLRs by dividing their highest quality capital, known as [Tier 1 Capital](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/t/tier1capital.asp#:~:text=Tier%201%20capital%20represents%20the,include%20noncumulative%2C%20nonredeemable%20preferred%20stock.)[23](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-23-156939084), by the dollar value of their exposures on and off their balance sheets[24](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-24-156939084), dubbed TLE (total leverage exposure) by Fed architects. In short, the higher the ratio, the more capital a bank needs to hold against the value of its assets.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pS0M!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe2dd9a20-ed96-45a8-a45c-9238c7d7106c_5760x3240.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pS0M!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe2dd9a20-ed96-45a8-a45c-9238c7d7106c_5760x3240.png)

Fed officials, using Basel III guidelines, have mandated large banks[25](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-25-156939084) to hold at least a 3% SLR, with balance sheets at both the commercial bank and dealer level impacting their ratio. G-SIBs (global systemically important banks), meanwhile, must maintain an extra buffer, the enhanced SLR (eSLR): an additional 2% buffer at the [BHC](https://www.fedpartnership.gov/bank-life-cycle/grow-shareholder-value/bank-holding-companies) (bank holding company) level and 3% buffer at the commercial bank level[26](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-26-156939084). As all Big Six dealer banks not only qualify as G-SIBs but serve as primary dealers hogging most Treasury market volumes, all major UST market makers remain bound by the harshest shackles. Out of its hundreds of subsidiaries, JPMorgan maintains over a 6% SLR at its primary commercial bank arm, JPMorgan Chase Bank N.A. — an insured depository institution (IDI)[27](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-27-156939084). In contrast, JPM’s BHC, including its giant primary dealer arm, JPMorgan Securities Inc, maintains over a 5% SLR.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nQvA!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1cc14eb3-c638-41d9-87f3-e2c0a8c90dbd_5760x3240.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!nQvA!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1cc14eb3-c638-41d9-87f3-e2c0a8c90dbd_5760x3240.png)

If the Fed exempts U.S. Treasuries or more from their SLRs, JPMorgan and other Big Six dealers will witness their TLE (total leverage exposure) fall while their capital levels remain unchanged[28](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-28-156939084). This should create “supplementary” room to expand their UST market making operations. At least, that’s what the consensus anticipates.

In practice, absent a severe plumbing episode, an SLR[29](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-29-156939084) exemption alone will fail to boost most dealers’ capacity to make markets significantly. Exempting U.S. Treasuries will only slightly help banks maintain their SLRs above and beyond mandatory levels, i.e. the SLR “danger zone”. Over many years, banks have already built [a considerable buffer](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/assessment-of-dealer-capacity-to-intermediate-in-treasury-and-agency-mbs-markets-20241022.html#721ad07d) above the SLR minimum to avoid a sudden [knock on the door](https://www.fdic.gov/regulations/examinations/enforcement-actions/ch-05.pdf#page=3) from officials. Falling below not only causes reputational damage, but offenders will be temporarily banned from issuing capital distributions — such as dividends and share buybacks[30](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-30-156939084). Banks therefore maintain abundant headroom, so large that exempting USTs will scarcely affect their dealers’ willingness to provide liquidity. Outright Treasury holdings make up only a slim portion of bank leverage, with the Fed revealing that USTs contribute around ~8% to bank assets. Conversely, almost all other exposures maintain a juicier weighting. Loans and leasings, for instance, comprise roughly a quarter of bank assets, while securities other than USTs average around 13%. Thus, it’s no surprise the SLR exemption during the COVID panic prompted no meaningful change in dealers’ ability to make a market in U.S. Treasuries.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ykby!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6e42efab-fdde-4fbb-a28e-e903603b8ba8_1967x1527.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ykby!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6e42efab-fdde-4fbb-a28e-e903603b8ba8_1967x1527.png)

Source/Credit: Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System, Consolidated Financial Statements for Holding Companies (Form FR Y-9C), [FEDS Notes](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/dealers-treasury-market-intermediation-and-the-supplementary-leverage-ratio-20230803.html), Paul Cochran, Sebastian Infante, Lubomir Petrasek, Zack Saravay, and Mary Tian

Altering the SLR while removing other plumbing barriers, however, will likely expand most Big Six dealers’ market-making capabilities. Depending on a bank’s business model, either risk-based or leverage-based capital will act as its “binding constraint”: jargon for the most restrictive rule. Over the past decade, the Big Six have achieved higher profitability with limited space by engineering their balance sheets to facilitate the “riskiest boring” businesses over “high volume, low margin” activities like market making. A big bank with a dealer arm, shackled by Dodd-Frank and Basel III, will still be incentivized to make markets but will prioritize traditional banking business[31](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-31-156939084). The latter entails risks[32](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-32-156939084) that mandate banks to hold more risk-based capital while “risk-blind” regulations like the SLR purposely ignore the same hazards. The capital a bank must hold to offset its balance sheet _risk_ — i.e. RWAs — thus could exceed the capital required to counteract its balance sheet _leverage_ — i.e. the SLR[33](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-33-156939084).

One activity that incurs risk-based charges happens to be the pivotal mechanism that dealers employ to make markets in USTs: [repo financing](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market). Despite an exemption of Treasuries from the SLR, dealers will still need to fund USTs and other assets via repos, which may require raising extra risk-based capital. Moreover, a great “dealer sheet congestion” has [emerged](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7f3318ea-61b5-495e-b048-3691f1242de0_3838x2620.png). Abundant supply and weak demand for USTs have forced primary dealers to absorb a large amount of inventory via repo. Meanwhile, the Fed’s QT and a steeper curve have also fueled [hedge fund trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-bilateral-repo-markets) that employ repo leverage obtained via dealers. Treasuries in the banking book have 0% risk weight, yet Treasuries held as trading assets of a dealer incur risk-based charges. Consequently, risk-based capital[34](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-34-156939084) has likely become the binding constraint in the combined dealer community when adding extra UST inventory. The SLR has served as an effective backstop to curb extreme leverage but does not act as the primary barrier for most market makers to expand their operations. Year after year, the Big Six have been “flip-flopping” between the SLR and RWA as their binding constraint. Back in 2022, only JPM and Morgan Stanley were bound by the SLR, and the rest by RWA. As of mid-2024, though, the SLR was binding for only Goldman and Morgan Stanley[35](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-35-156939084).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!AGzV!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F29d36e1b-d4db-4221-a74e-55426cdafabf_2712x1246.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!AGzV!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F29d36e1b-d4db-4221-a74e-55426cdafabf_2712x1246.png)

Source/Credit: FR Y−9C, FFIEC 101, [FEDS Notes](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/assessment-of-dealer-capacity-to-intermediate-in-treasury-and-agency-mbs-markets-20241022.html), Paul Cochran, Lubomir Petrasek, Zack Saravay, Mary Tian, and Edward Wu

With all major dealers holding significant headroom above their SLR “danger zones” and most constrained primarily by risk-based capital, a meaningful liberation of the Treasury plumbing will require much more alchemy than a SLR exemption. The exposures that officials choose to reduce or exempt from not only the SLR but other regulations matter. The plumbing is nuanced, and banks won’t absorb more USTs if the Fed merely exempts them along with reserves, let alone will the Big Six buy a lot more Treasuries in their non-trading portfolios with the space created[36](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-36-156939084). Exempting USTs in trading books[37](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-37-156939084) and repos from risk-based capital ratios — such as the tier 1 capital ratio, not the SLR, will expand most dealers’ UST market making capacity, but that goes against the core tenets of Basel III[38](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-38-156939084).

Fed officials should instead create an SLR “on and off switch,” exempting USTs plus reserves whenever a “[dash for cash](https://www.bankofengland.co.uk/speech/2022/february/jon-cunliffe-keynote-address-fia-sifma-asset-management-derivatives-forum)”-style episode re-emerges. This switch will provide the Fed with (yet another) “plumbing put”, reigniting the willingness of dealers to step in and make markets in USTs, which central bankers can't turn to once a permanent SLR exemption is in place. But even when this switch is turned on, the Fed faces another obstacle. Once officials loosen capital constraints, they must still contend with dealers’ internal risk limits set in-house and not via regulation, limits that force Treasury desks to pull back from market making without emotion. The barriers to truly liberate the Treasury market’s plumbing post-GFC remain substantial, so much so that the Fed’s emergency OMOs (open market operations) likely remain the sole instrument that will keep USTs “well greased” in all scenarios.

But don’t tell that to markets. Deregulatory hype is here and increasing, while a regulatory “rug pull”[39](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i#footnote-39-156939084) — a failure of monetary leaders to pass adequate (or any) plumbing relief — is very much still on the table.
