Remember, nothing in the piece is financial advice. If you act on anything, you agree to this [disclaimer](https://www.conks.plumbing/about#%C2%A7disclaimer). Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber!**_

# The Fed's Relief Valve: Part II - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii
> an attempt to grease the Treasury market's plumbing may fall short

---
— _click [here](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i) for Part I and [here](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii-pro) for Pro_

The hype over the Fed opening its “relief valve,” an easing of constraints impeding the largest UST (U.S. Treasury) market makers, has reached its peak. From a [slowing](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/files/monetary20250319a1.pdf) of the Fed’s QT to officials [hinting](https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/speech/bowman20250205a.htm) at a relaxation of dealers’ shackles, numerous catalysts briefly prompted markets (i.e. swap spreads[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-1-157878418)) to price in a “plumbing liberation”, one set to grease the Treasury market’s inner workings. This move, however, was quickly superseded by a regulatory “rug pull”, a [sharp reversal](https://pbs.twimg.com/media/GoCnaFRXoAACmJF?format=jpg&name=medium) in swap spreads spurred first by the longstanding pressures weighing on U.S. sovereign debt, plus a significant unwind in positioning. Panic produced by Trump’s “tariff cascade” then dealt the killing blow, as market participants began to genuinely question U.S. Treasuries as a global reserve asset. Desks engaging in key trades[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-2-157878418) that fuel UST liquidity encountered mild stress, while long-end bid-ask spreads somewhat [widened](https://pbs.twimg.com/media/GoEbHk1bwAI-4U3?format=png&name=medium). The multipolar world and dollar doom narratives appeared (somewhat) feasible for the first time in decades.

But Trump’s capitulation to bond market volatility soon followed, prompting market participants to turn their focus back toward an incoming deregulatory agenda. An increasing number of leading officials have suggested easing [the Fed’s SLR](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-slr) as the U.S. central bank’s primary relief valve mechanism. Nevertheless, this and other proposed modifications will fail to grease “the pipes” of the Treasury market as much as they anticipate. Numerous pressures, both political and technical, will continue to impede the UST plumbing.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zLZ_!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5307fd2c-77e2-45d4-a477-fc4f09bcf53e_5775x3162.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zLZ_!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5307fd2c-77e2-45d4-a477-fc4f09bcf53e_5775x3162.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop)

Swap spreads, or simply “spreads” in market lingo, which directly capture the tenacity of dealers’ regulatory constraints, have adapted accordingly — [downwards](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Facbab5eb-2f04-45bc-abee-ebd5eee37e32_5441x3865.png). A bigger Fed relief valve is needed.

Ever since Fed Chair Powell indicated “the agencies” — the collective name for the Fed Board, the OCC, and the FDIC — could finally be devising a plan to relieve major banks of balance sheet constraints, markets have been pricing out (_not in_) intense deregulatory hype. Spreads capturing costs to intermediate USTs versus interest rate swaps have re-tightened sharply (i.e. fallen)[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-3-157878418) after widening upon several SLR-centric comments from officials — the latter move being crowned (too soon) as the “[trade of the year](https://www.risk.net/markets/7961167/hedge-funds-flock-to-us-swap-spreads-on-slr-easing-talk)” within the hedge fund community. Despite the latest brief recovery fueled by a reversal in bearish positioning, then boosted by both Bessent and Deputy Treasury Sec Faulkender [advocating](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2025-04-15/treasury-s-no-2-says-conversations-being-had-on-slr-us-bonds) for SLR relief, the long-term status quo of increasingly negative spreads has endured, exposing underlying plumbing tensions.

Minus any frictions, market participants should force yields on U.S. Treasuries and rates on swaps of the same maturity to trade in line or at a positive spread, as repo markets allow participants to construct a synthetic swap (e.g. a UST long position funded via [repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market)). Yet, numerous obstacles stand in the way of the most prominent players in swaps. Spreads have historically traded positive and widened[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-4-157878418) (i.e. the blue area below), as balance sheets were once elastic enough to exploit any opportunity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3jmA!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd537a62b-dfb0-48b6-bbf9-d52e9be1e3c3_2298x1594.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!3jmA!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd537a62b-dfb0-48b6-bbf9-d52e9be1e3c3_2298x1594.png)

Source/Credit: Federal Reserve, [Economic Policy Review](https://www.newyorkfed.org/medialibrary/media/research/epr/2018/epr_2018_negative-swap-spreads_boyarchenko.pdf), Nina Boyarchenko, Pooja Gupta, Nick Steele, and Jacqueline Yen

Alas, a subprime meltdown altered the status quo. In reaction to the private sector's inability to manufacture safe assets pre-GFC, the global regulatory complex devised the “secured standard” (_Conks’_ shorthand for [Dodd-Frank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dodd%E2%80%93Frank_Wall_Street_Reform_and_Consumer_Protection_Act), [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm), and [SEC reforms](https://www.ici.org/viewpoints/25-view-mmf-reforms)), gradually imposing tougher leverage and risk limits on banks, the primary dealer arms of which operate the most extensive swap desks. Soon enough, spreads went negative, with the SLR becoming the major source of negativity, not as the cause but as an obstacle impeding dealers from arbitraging spreads between swaps and USTs, both close substitutes.

Before the U.S. implementation of the Basel III leverage ratio, known fondly by global banks as the SLR, only their internal risk limits were a major barrier to arbitraging swap spreads — a once-popular trade in which desks could pick up approximately 10bps of carry with little capital upfront. But then, the global regulatory complex intervened. Regulators slowly phased in the SLR, a non-risk-weighted leverage ratio that swiftly reduced dealers’ willingness to engage in spreads trades.

[

![ (1 of 6)](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!wCVM!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdec08a46-2cab-493f-a35b-e1370771f1e8_6240x3510.png " (1 of 6)")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!wCVM!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdec08a46-2cab-493f-a35b-e1370771f1e8_6240x3510.png)

As before, banks targeted, on average, a 15% return on equity ([ROE](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/r/returnonequity.asp)), ideally allocating capital to trades yielding a fruitful return. The post-2008 regulatory agenda, however, decimated bank ROEs, especially among the largest dealers. As each exposure was captured by the SLR’s denominator: TLE (total leverage exposure), this boosted the numerator: a bank’s required ([Tier 1](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/t/tier1capital.asp#:~:text=Tier%201%20capital%20represents%20the,include%20noncumulative%2C%20nonredeemable%20preferred%20stock.)) capital. The 3% SLR minimum imposed by officials required U.S. banks to target a much larger, negative spread to achieve the same outcome. And the more systemically important the dealer bank, the less inviting spread trades became. G-SIBs (global systemically important banks) had to satisfy a 5-6% SLR.

[

![ (2 of 6)](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YBCr!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d63a35e-8fc0-4f6d-94b1-3eb948d823f8_6240x3510.png " (2 of 6)")

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!YBCr!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7d63a35e-8fc0-4f6d-94b1-3eb948d823f8_6240x3510.png)

Fast forward to today, the Basel III leverage ratio’s wrath is still the greatest for the most systemic players, who’ve retreated from eliminating (now former) dislocations in the spreads market. Not only is a negative swap spread considered a feature, not a bug, but other structural forces have produced a “great tightening” in the spreads market. The number of catalysts creating downward pressure on swap spreads has reached new extremes, with the most recent force being the aftereffects of COVID. Products of the U.S. government’s massive fiscal injections have tested the Treasury market’s plumbing to its limits. An immense supply of USTs paired with a colossal deficit has pushed 30-year swap spreads toward a whopping -100 basis points, a mirror image of the early 2000s.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zay4!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Facbab5eb-2f04-45bc-abee-ebd5eee37e32_5441x3865.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!zay4!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Facbab5eb-2f04-45bc-abee-ebd5eee37e32_5441x3865.png)

negative swap spreads persist across the spreads curve

But now, in what _Conks_ calls _[The SLR Rework](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-regulatory-rework)_, monetary leaders have decided it’s finally time to alleviate regulatory shackles on financial behemoths. Even so, their ability to effectively juice Treasury market liquidity isn’t guaranteed. The proposed rework may also fail to produce a persistent reversal (widening) in swap spreads, regardless of a few jolts higher that remove a chunk out of spread negativity. Despite a [massive effort](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-evolution) to fortify the Treasury market’s internals, swap spreads indicate officials will likely need to ratchet up their deregulatory agenda beyond leverage-based relief, further boosting the market-making capacity of major dealers, especially the “Big Six”[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-5-157878418).

As _Conks_ and others have [demonstrated](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i), relaxing only the SLR will prove inadequate. Top U.S. officials have come out in droves to announce their support for a leverage ratio “easing”, but none have expanded their horizons. While U.S. heads of monetary state solely mention the SLR, Fed researchers [concede](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/dealers-treasury-market-intermediation-and-the-supplementary-leverage-ratio-20230803.html) that most UST market makers are bound by a string of other restraints. As demonstrated in _[Part I](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-i)_, most of the Big Six dealers that dominate UST volumes must hold a greater amount of capital to satisfy the risk, not the leverage, they have taken on.

For dealer banks to absorb additional UST supply, they must raise capital to account for all exposures captured by the SLR, such as the Treasury itself, and if engaging in a swap spread trade, cash flows from the interest rate swap and any margin and haircuts incurred[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-6-157878418). However, they also rack up risk-based capital costs, including the [G-SIB surcharge](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/ccyb_gsib/index.htm)[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-7-157878418), which mandates the largest dealer banks to hold more equity whenever increasing their arsenal of USTs — including those funded via repo. Despite an SLR rework and subsequent alleviation of leverage-based limits, the Fed’s overall capital complex remains unforgiving.

After combining all other bank business, risk-based capital (within a gargantuan U.S. bank capital complex seen above) is likely preventing the dealer bank community from willingly absorbing more USTs compared to the SLR, with risk-weighted assets (RWAs) acting as their binding constraint.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!m3Pe!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F71e025d2-1e46-4d97-bec2-f2307d3b2ea8_5775x3162.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!m3Pe!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F71e025d2-1e46-4d97-bec2-f2307d3b2ea8_5775x3162.png)

Monetary leaders must, therefore, relieve other shackles to achieve a significant “greasing” of the Treasury market’s plumbing. To truly relieve frictions encountered by market-makers in USTs, regulators will likely have to exempt U.S. sovereign debt from _both_ risk-based (RWA) and leverage-based (SLR) capital requirements. These actions, of course, partially undo a decade or more of public and private regulatory measures and standards, as well as conflicts between financial regulators and banking giants. Even so, with a pro-deregulatory leadership both inside and outside the Fed now applying pressure, anything is possible. The potential types of regulatory modifications remain abundant, yet the margin for error is slim.

At first glance, a fix appears straightforward. Treasury market liquidity has withered (without collapsing entirely), so reducing the potency of the SLR could assist market makers in intermediating U.S. Treasuries. The past decade, however, has revealed numerous failed attempts at a rework. Complex political and technical forces have hindered any progress. Presently, the SLR and risk-based capital requirements, such as the RWA (risk-weighted asset) standard, function as the Fed initially intended. Most banks have rewired operations to maximize risk within regulatory limits. And if, for any (unlikely) reason, a bank decides to optimize its balance sheet for leverage-based (i.e. high volume, low margin) operations, the SLR becomes binding once again. Thus, despite calls for a shakeup, no major initiative to revamp the SLR has arisen.

That is, until now. With POTUS and Bessent in charge, the second Trump era looks to be the turning point. Also striving to deregulate wherever desired, the Fed’s Michelle Bowman will presumably be crowned the Fed’s next Vice Chair of Supervision. Where to start and what to adjust is where the challenge begins. Back in 2018, some federal agencies [proposed](https://www.federalregister.gov/documents/2018/04/19/2018-08066/regulatory-capital-rules-regulatory-capital-enhanced-supplementary-leverage-ratio-standards-for-us) modifying the eSLR by reducing its strength, yet no changes ever materialized. If the agencies were to resurrect their 2018 proposal today, they would force G-SIBs to hold only half of their surcharge instead of preserving a 2% fixed eSLR buffer.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ricm!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3480d1a0-7cdc-4493-bf4a-c6e756f3ef6b_5775x2887.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ricm!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3480d1a0-7cdc-4493-bf4a-c6e756f3ef6b_5775x2887.png)

Thus, the Big Six — all running large swaps desks and obeying the eSLR — will witness their leverage-based capital requirements fall relative to risk-based capital requirements, either removing or preventing leverage-based capital from acting as their binding constraint. A more direct approach would involve cutting the entire SLR in half, including the eSLR buffer, ensuring all UST market makers remain bound by the risk-based capital complex[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-8-157878418). But if it were that easy, the agencies would have used it. In reality, the “simple” solution would face too much opposition from pro-regulatory advocates.

A few years after the 2018 proposal vanished, a more palatable solution emerged. COVID-19 prompted the Fed to exempt U.S. Treasuries and bank reserves entirely from the (e)SLR, [initiating](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-reverse-repo-endgame) a great shedding of excess deposits from the banking system and a $2.5 trillion Fed RRP buildup. Once the exemption expired, Fed officials [invited](https://www.federalreserve.gov/newsevents/pressreleases/bcreg20210319a.htm) comments on modifications to the SLR, an effort that quickly faded into the shadows. To this day, nothing has come of it. Nevertheless, a “COVID-19 replica” — excluding Treasuries and reserves from the SLR — has now been touted as the primary resolution[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-relief-valve-part-ii#footnote-9-157878418).

Regulators will likely stick to their guns and choose the easiest, plainest solution. The megabanks have been fighting to remove all sovereign liabilities from the SLR for some time, and they will likely get their wish under the latest admin. _Conks_ expects merely an SLR “carve-out”, removing sovereign liabilities (USTs and bank reserves) from the SLR, the smallest modification. This will likely only go into effect when volatility rises enough for dealers to send SOS calls to the Fed’s money market desk. Meanwhile, _Conks_ proposes that the Fed also exempt Treasuries held by a bank’s dealer subsidiary from risk-based capital requirements. Yet, to force U.S. monetary officials to modify more than the SLR, a great regulatory “capitulation” must transpire.

The latest deregulatory agenda could result in such a rework. Multiple political barriers do stand between a plumbing liberation and the continuation of the status quo. However, as we’ve seen with the recent Trump-Powell feud, some rules are meant to be broken, or in this case, shattered. At some point, risk-based capital relief via a regulatory capitulation will emerge.

Though long before this happens, whether it takes six months or a decade, negative spreads will persist. In _Part I_, _Conks_ successfully captured a tightening move. Now, an opportunity to capture another unwind in deregulatory plumbing hype has emerged via a widening (rise) in swap spreads. As witnessed with the latest Bessent comments, any SLR relief talk will cause spreads to rebound, providing an opportunity to continue to ride the negative spread status quo. Short-term, the only way is up for UST issuance, which is weighing on dealer balance sheets. Meanwhile, despite Trump’s protests of a large fiscal deficit, the masses will protest against any spending cuts. That spells wider bases and declining (tighter) swap spreads. Until a regulatory capitulation surfaces, look out below!

# The Fed's Reckoning: Part I - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search
> a plumbing "soft (or hard) landing" awaits monetary leaders

---
— click [here](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/pro-the-feds-reckoning-part-one) for _Pro_

After a series of pauses and near misses, the “excess cash” era is finally coming to an end. The Fed’s arsenal to defer the next money market rates upheaval, in order to reduce its balance sheet as much as possible, has been depleted. Upon an expected violent end to funding markets the previous year, the Fed has already [lowered](https://www.bloomberg.com/news/articles/2024-12-18/fed-tweaks-rate-on-key-rrp-policy-tool-for-first-time-since-2021) its o/n RRP (overnight reverse repo)[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-1-173191047) rate (by 5bps) to the bottom of its target range, boosting reserve balances and suppressing broader money market volatility. The U.S. central bank then solidified its “upper jaw”, the SRF ([standing repo facility](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-standing-repo)), offering morning _and_ afternoon auctions[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-2-173191047), thereby placing a stronger cap on money market rates[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-3-173191047). The window before banks try to venture into markets that monetary leaders do not want them to tread[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-4-173191047) has thus been extended.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Pz24!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb8c3f768-ba3a-4486-b946-af8c56023026_8100x5850.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Pz24!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb8c3f768-ba3a-4486-b946-af8c56023026_8100x5850.png)

pure reserves are those sitting in the reserve accounts at the Fed, while neutralized reserves were previously stored as reverse repos in the Fed’s RRP

This gap has widened even further after the Fed, reacting to potential blind spots arising from increasingly volatile money market flows[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-5-173191047), has partially unwound QT, downgrading the “threat level” of balance sheet runoff (i.e. the effects of reducing its balance sheet on asset prices) to neutral. Even so, with the Fed now shredding “pure” reserves — those straight out of banks’ reserve accounts, not “neutralized” in the Fed’s RRP[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-6-173191047) _(see above)_, officials must eventually initiate their next plumbing bazooka and restart liquidity injections. The challenge, however, is in the timing. _The Fed’s Reckoning_ is approaching.

The Fed’s RRP balance hitting zero has been widely touted as a major liquidity bottleneck. Instead, it merely reveals a banking system able to handle all remaining reserves without the Fed needing to soak up cash that banks won’t (or can’t) hold because of regulatory constraints. With Basel III restricting leverage (i.e. balance sheet size) after COVID-19 relief expired[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-7-173191047), banks had to force $2.5 trillion of depositor money into the U.S. central bank’s shock absorber[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-8-173191047) . Upon levying fees or imposing negative rates, the largest banking giants drove trillions of excess cash into money funds (MMFs), which had no choice but to invest heavily in overnight reverse repos (o/n RRPs) with the Fed[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-9-173191047). This involved asset management arms of the largest financial players converting bank deposits into Fed RRPs. Reserves and deposits fell, as MMF shares and the Fed’s RRP balance ballooned. As the latest hiking cycle and UST market (U.S. Treasury) turmoil ended in mid-2023, however, enough bill supply and private repo demand materialized to drive trillions of dollars out of the Fed’s RRP and — back — into the banking system.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!jSaH!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F44c1ecc1-ec12-4b4c-a9a5-8b913a943e8f_5400x3665.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!jSaH!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F44c1ecc1-ec12-4b4c-a9a5-8b913a943e8f_5400x3665.png)

The unwinding of Fed RRPs has initiated the release of reserves previously rejected by banks, which have replaced interbank liquidity lost to the Fed’s QT[10](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-10-173191047) (quantitative tightening). De-neutralization, i.e. the unwinding of Fed RRPs and the subsequent release of neutralized reserves, occurs when private sector alternatives — mainly repos and 1-month t-bills — are issued and grow relatively attractive to central bank offerings, usually as repo rates and 1-month bill yields rise a few basis points above o/n RRP (the rate the Fed pays on its RRPs).

For years, the Fed’s shock absorber has counteracted falling balances in banks’ Fed master (reserve) accounts[11](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-11-173191047), acting as a protective layer against significant money market turmoil. But no longer. The banking system’s “LcLoR”, Fed-speak for the “lowest comfortable level of reserves”, draws closer. Volumes at the [repo hierarchy’s](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-repo-hierarchy) upper layers — namely, repos in which banks deploy and thus drain reserves and o/n (overnight) repos between dealers and the Fed via the SRF — keep climbing, the earliest signs of lackluster money market liquidity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cYek!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1abf8fcf-359b-4874-890c-5c10d59982fb_5238x5500.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cYek!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1abf8fcf-359b-4874-890c-5c10d59982fb_5238x5500.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop & available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-repo-hierarchy))

Yet, even with zero RRPs, reserves are too plentiful for any rate eruption to surface. Jackson Hole was a green light for rate cuts but a plumbing nothingburger. The September tax date and large UST settlements due on the 15th, meanwhile, may also result in a temporary scare — a quasi-quarter-end. Central bank officials, though, will quickly dismiss any hint of a _faux_ repo crisis. Fed leaders desire to shed as many unwanted assets from the Fed’s balance sheet, “reloading” their primary weapon, ready to vacuum up fresh duration (i.e. interest rate) risk during the next black swan event. The most recent, impressive Fed manoeuvre has been the sheer delay tactics employed to reduce the balance sheet and maintain runoff. The Fed has pulled out all the stops. Regardless, interbank liquidity now has to fall. Pure reserves must be used to clear interbank markets, as the U.S. central bank’s shock absorber has run dry. ~$400 billion in reserves now lies between the Fed and a “hard landing”, where a surprise Fed put will send asset markets higher while creating another motive to “cut back” on rate cuts.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Lgjd!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa23164b9-6108-49e2-8466-eca04d715b3e_4645x3227.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Lgjd!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa23164b9-6108-49e2-8466-eca04d715b3e_4645x3227.png)

A brief “excess collateral” era — the opposite of an excess cash era — will now begin to push up dollar funding rates within the Fed’s target band, threatening to terminate the Fed’s balance sheet reduction. Paired with superior models and foresight from past [shaky episodes](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-repo-defensive-part-one), monetary leaders’ chances of preventing “repocalypse 2.0” have risen dramatically. Nevertheless, through a series of complex interlinkages and mechanisms, rates in various money markets — both onshore and offshore — are poised to climb and remain elevated for the first time in years. With the goal of achieving maximum balance sheet reduction in mind[12](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-treasury-market-black-swan?utm_source=publication-search#footnote-12-173191047), the Fed’s willingness to pull off a “plumbing soft landing” is about to be tested…

_…the mechanics of which will be unveiled in Part II._

_**If you enjoyed this, hit the ♡ button below the footnotes to let us know (it helps us grow) and share a link/message on social media. Comments are also encouraged. Remember, nothing in the piece is financial advice. If you act on anything, you agree to this [disclaimer](https://www.conks.plumbing/about#%C2%A7disclaimer). Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber! For more, check out [Pro](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/conks-pro).**_

# The Fed's Reckoning: Part II - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii
> money market interlinkages and the coming central bank put

---
— _click here for [Part I](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-one) and here for_ _[Pro](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/pro-the-feds-reckoning-part-two)_

The decision facing monetary leaders that could ultimately avert a “plumbing hard landing” grows nearer. Just as most Fed RRPs[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-1-173354458) have been unwound, marking the end of an excess cash era[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-2-173354458), money markets have faced their first significant hurdle. While the U.S. banking system keeps slowly approaching its lowest comfortable level of reserves (a.k.a LCLoR), market participants have witnessed a mid-month plunge in liquidity from corporations withdrawing cash from MMFs[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-3-173354458) to fund tax payments, just as dealer banks[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-4-173354458) deployed more reserves into [repo markets](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) to clear massive mid-month UST (U.S. Treasury) settlements. Even so, the mid-month funding episode created little fear over a replay of the September 2019 repo spike, i.e. the [repocalypse](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-repo-defensive-part-one), where repo rates, overnight Fed Funds[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-5-173354458) (o/n FF), and FX swaps breached the U.S. central bank’s “upper jaws,” i.e. the top of its local/global [target ranges](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-global-dollar-rates-ef1). A near-empty Fed RRP facility, paired with a corporate tax date and large UST settlements, was no match for a system still flush with liquidity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Gy1W!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4665f24-2424-4cdf-b6f4-b81587a31979_4461x3291.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Gy1W!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb4665f24-2424-4cdf-b6f4-b81587a31979_4461x3291.png)

1-month bills were busy “sniffing out” cuts

The mid-month turn also coincided with the end of a much-feared “TGA refill”, a restocking of the U.S. government’s bank account — completed ahead of schedule. Treasury officials’ $850 billion TGA stockpile will now enable the U.S. empire to cover all unexpected threats to its payment flows, but at the expense of banks’ capacity to settle interbank flows, as government revenues lower reserve balances of the largest banks, G-SIBs[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-6-173354458), the major U.S. dollar clearers. The antidote will soon be an increase in daylight overdrafts, Fed lingo for intraday credit[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-7-173354458) supplied automatically when banks’ reserve accounts hit zero, an upsurge of which (from smaller banks) has become a key covert input[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-8-173354458) in the Fed’s reserve scarcity “[alarm system](https://x.com/conksresearch/status/1972980249026814141?s=46)”. That, alongside other rising pressures in funding markets, will soon apply upward pressure on interbank rates, observable via a rising [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr), the “canary in the coalmine” for the Fed to respond with a long-awaited plumbing put.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!V4fv!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6696b5fe-8445-4d1a-82bb-3972032b105d_3744x3744.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!V4fv!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6696b5fe-8445-4d1a-82bb-3972032b105d_3744x3744.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop - available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-global-dollar-rates-ef1))

Rising interbank rates, however, are just a few of the Fed’s concerns. Reserves clear all money markets in the global dollar hierarchy, not just repos and o/n FF. As one rising rate pushes other rates higher along with it, the upper levels of hierarchy will likewise soon feel the drag of waning interbank liquidity. For the first time in years, the level at which dollar funding markets clear will rise and remain elevated, lasting until the Fed u-turns and fully initiates a _[Great Rebalancing](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-great-rebalancing)_[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-9-173354458)_,_ an attempt to prepare its balance sheet for the next COVID-style event. Nevertheless, officials also remain in pursuit of a max balance sheet reduction, just as a complex web of money market interlinkages grows increasingly volatile. The U.S. central bank’s capacity to avoid another repo upheaval is thus about to be challenged. With its inventory of delay tactics now exhausted, _the Fed’s Reckoning —_ its decision to let money markets “run hot” or prematurely deploy its next plumbing put — is looming, and the intricacies of dollar funding markets may hold the answer.

Over the last decade or so, market participants have witnessed a grand experiment in monetary alchemy. The superabundance of reserves unleashed by the Fed ever since the Great Financial Crisis (GFC) has rendered the U.S. central bank’s old system of setting interest rates, its so-called [corridor system](https://libertystreeteconomics.newyorkfed.org/2012/04/corridors-and-floors-in-monetary-policy/), ineffective. With slight changes in the level of reserve balances no longer affecting Fed Funds, officials could simply set a target range in which not only o/n FF but most onshore and offshore dollar rates traded. Basel III came online shortly after, outlawing banking giants from deploying unlimited balance sheet to arbitrage away (soon-to-be former) mispricings. From then on, money market rates grew fragmented, forming layers in order of plumbing frictions. A new, global dollar rates hierarchy emerged, and at its outer edges lay the Fed’s global and local “jaws”: the U.S. central bank’s offshore (global) and onshore (local) target ranges for U.S. dollar rates.

Forming its local jaws, the Fed’s standing repo facility (SRF) has served as the guardian of the north, while its o/n RRP facility serves as the protector of the south. The Fed’s IORB (interest on reserve balances)[10](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-10-173354458) and o/n RRP have provided additional levers to contain money market rates, via what officials call “technical adjustments”: nudging IORB or o/n RRP up or down, usually by 5bps at a time. The Fed’s swap line network, meanwhile (a.k.a its global dollar backstop), has formed its global upper jaw, while a global lower jaw has yet to be implemented. For now, the Fed’s RRP facility contains downward pressure on both onshore and offshore $ rates globally[11](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-11-173354458).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2GUZ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F82986ea4-e136-4f4f-bf6b-81fe8fa854fa_6747x5053.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!2GUZ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F82986ea4-e136-4f4f-bf6b-81fe8fa854fa_6747x5053.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop - available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-plumbing-of-global))

Despite their effectiveness, however, in challenging and volatile times, the Fed has had no choice but to strengthen its jaws. In 2015, in response to a significant safe asset shortage caused by rising reserve balances and a lack of bill issuance, the Fed reinforced its lower boundaries. Money funds (MMFs) flush with cash ran out of short-term liquid instruments and began to bid down money market rates too low for the Fed’s liking, prompting (after experimentation) the creation of an official o/n RRP facility. At the top of its local jaws, an official SRF (standing repo facility) — offering o/n Fed repos — was constructed from the ashes of the [2019 repocalypse](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-repo-defensive-part-one). Deficient interbank liquidity and volatile flows prompted the Fed to convert its temporary repo “OMOs” (open market operations) into a standing facility. Yet, it took three long years for another year-end funding squeeze to emerge for monetary leaders to introduce o/n Fed repos with morning settlement[12](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-12-173354458). This not only boosted intraday repo liquidity, but provided the Fed a bigger cushion between banks tapping reserves via the SRF and banks venturing (too deep) and applying pressure on rates in the o/n FF market. These upgrades have enabled the U.S. central bank to hold rates almost effortlessly within its jaws, except in one scenario: an era of excess collateral.

Since its response to COVID-19, the Fed’s job has not been to keep rates from skyrocketing, but rather to prevent them from falling too far. The sheer level of liquidity from COVID operations sent o/n repo rates even below the Fed’s lower bound of its target range, unveiling “leaky” but manageable lower jaws. This time around, o/n rates have shifted toward the Fed’s upper limits and will continue to rise as cash becomes scarcer relative to collateral in the system. Excess collateral regimes are irregular and short, but never sweet. It’s where monetary (plumbing) policy will always be restrictive. Although the system requires a large overall balance sheet to operate in the post-GFC era, the Fed has imposed austerity via prolonged QT to remove as many unnecessary assets as possible. The only reason a plumbing bazooka hasn’t been initiated sooner is because of a debt limit drama and a $2.5 trillion hoard of unwinding RRPs, offsetting reserves lost to Fed QT — a delayed return to reserve scarcity.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!OOTM!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2f985e40-9e2f-4937-8d67-815ca405eaf6_4369x3100.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!OOTM!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2f985e40-9e2f-4937-8d67-815ca405eaf6_4369x3100.png)

a TGA refill (yellow) and the unwind of Fed RRPs (light grey and blue) both offset reserves (red) destroyed via balance sheet reduction, but no longer…

But now, the Fed’s delay tactics have run out. The height of the 2024 funding mania has already forced the Fed to lower o/n RRP by 5bps and introduce morning Fed repos. Central bank leaders still have more options. Yet, based on past excess collateral regimes, it’s unlikely they will deploy any remaining weapons. Unlike its 5bps cut to o/n RRP in late ‘24, officials won’t likely administer any cuts to IORB to contain money market rates. Before the 2019 repo spike — which marked the end of the last excess collateral era — officials attempted to lure banks into deploying their excess cash into bills by lowering the rate paid on reserves, which should have dampened money market rates. Back then, IORB was known as IOER (interest on excess reserves), where Federal Funds (the official name for bank reserves) held by banks above the pre-GFC system’s “required reserves”[13](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-13-173354458) were considered excess. Today’s “excess” reserves are balances held in banks’ reserve accounts that exceed those used to satisfy liquidity requirements[14](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-14-173354458). Instead, cuts to IORB failed to entice banks to deploy the new system’s interpretation of excess reserves into money markets. The “Big Six” banks[15](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-15-173354458), primarily JPMorgan, not only deployed liquidity into “juicier” assets, but more importantly, excess reserves also fled the Fed account of JPM, the private lender of last resort and the _aficionado_ of containing money market rates before the Fed must be warned. The Fed’s IORB cuts redistributed reserves to other Big Six banks not possessing the same expertise, reducing JPM’s ability to poach, thus increasing the chances of a repo spike — and the rest is [history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/September_2019_events_in_the_U.S._repo_market). Bank of America’s reserve stockpile was comparable in size to JPM's. Yet, the traders who wielded JPM’s stockpile had much more power and access to fix market imbalances.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4p99!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F616f16be-54c9-4782-9a77-d981ed3f8d9a_4435x3169.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!4p99!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F616f16be-54c9-4782-9a77-d981ed3f8d9a_4435x3169.png)

As of today, the “JPM Cushion”, the gap between JPM’s reserves today and pre-repocalypse levels, is dwindling. Officials, however, won’t make the same blunder again. If they do decide to lower IORB, it will be alongside further cuts to o/n RRP, as one last-ditch attempt to delay a QT ending.

Delay tactics have run dry, even though more technically exist! The reserve drain must continue. The $2.5 trillion wall of o/n Fed RRPs, once acting as a protective layer, is approaching the zero bound. A positive TGCR-RRP spread[16](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-16-173354458) _(see below)_ has widened enough to force money funds to either rotate into higher-yielding bills or private repos, and for so long that the Fed’s RRP [balance](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/RRPONTSYD) has sunk to nil. Since every dollar of net bill issuance has led to a RRP drain of roughly half the magnitude, and mammoth issuance is set to prevail, an empty Fed facility is not a matter of if but when.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!mmgM!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbc9b5265-f864-4b2e-a792-4b442df4bd4e_4392x3264.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!mmgM!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbc9b5265-f864-4b2e-a792-4b442df4bd4e_4392x3264.png)

Soon, only “pure” reserves[17](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-17-173354458) will clear dollar funding markets — i.e. repos, bills, Eurodollars, and FX swaps — and, unfortunately for officials, interbank funding markets too — i.e. o/n FF. The slow bleed toward the banking system’s lowest comfortable level of reserves continues as QT nibbles away at banks’ Fed accounts. The level at which money markets clear is consequently shifting higher, and soon every layer of the dollar funding hierarchy will be activated.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!V4fv!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6696b5fe-8445-4d1a-82bb-3972032b105d_3744x3744.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!V4fv!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F6696b5fe-8445-4d1a-82bb-3972032b105d_3744x3744.png)

same graphic as above, added again for convenience

With the Fed’s excess cash absorber almost empty, private players, not the Fed, now dictate rates at which funding markets clear. The floor is shifting higher because we’re in the early innings of reserve scarcity, and that floor lies between the midpoint at which market makers (dealers and banks) lend and borrow. For instance, hundreds of billions in volume can trade above the cheapest Fed repo rate while dealers still make a positive spread (say 20bps by borrowing at 4.55% from another dealer and lending to a hedge fund at 4.75%) while also avoiding the stigma of tapping central bank liquidity[18](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-18-173354458). As reserves diminish, dealer banks, once primarily flush with cash, will be flush with collateral — exacerbated, among other frictions, by ballooning fiscal deficits. More layers of the dollar (funding) hierarchy are not only activating but also changing the interplay between prominent money market participants. During the COVID era of “reserve superabundance”, money funds were so awash with cash that they “paid off” dealers by lending them dollars at rates below o/n RRP — the Fed’s risk-free repo rate. Now the tables have turned. Dealers must compete for money fund dollars — and hedge fund dollars, as cash levels continue to tighten on a day-to-day basis.

Dealers (and banks) must decide which, out of a multitude of markets, to raise and lend this cash into, and those markets make up most of the levels in the dollar hierarchy.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-dSQ!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F71cf8ee3-a40d-47ef-b95c-c1f2bfe2b67d_4433x3218.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-dSQ!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F71cf8ee3-a40d-47ef-b95c-c1f2bfe2b67d_4433x3218.png)

On the funding side, they can tap o/n TPR (triparty repo in yellow), obtaining cash secured against collateral mostly from money funds — the major providers of cash, or o/n GCF (the interdealer market in green), obtaining cash secured against collateral mostly from fellow dealers and banks. Plus, if you happen to be a custodian bank or foreign bank with the least regulatory burdens, you can tap o/n Fed Funds (the interbank market), obtaining reserves from FHLBs (Federal Home Loan Banks)[19](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-19-173354458) to deploy into either repos or FX swaps for a higher return. A dealer’s funding options, even within the Basel III era, remain abundant.

On the lending side, dealers must then seek the most lucrative spreads. Money markets are complex beings. Hierarchies exist within hierarchies and interplay with others, like galaxies colliding, with the most critical hierarchy found in repo markets.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cYek!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1abf8fcf-359b-4874-890c-5c10d59982fb_5238x5500.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!cYek!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F1abf8fcf-359b-4874-890c-5c10d59982fb_5238x5500.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop - available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-repo-hierarchy))

Triparty repo (o/n TPR) is where dealers fund their lending to other dealers in the interdealer market (o/n GCF) and to hedge funds in the dealer-to-customer market (o/n DVP). These rates combine to form SOFR, the primary benchmark for secured dollar lending globally. Once SOFR rises consistently above interbank rates such as o/n FF and IORB, market participants begin to alter their behavior. With IORB, banks deploy reserves into repos as these yield more than IORB, which applies pressure to rates in the interbank market when smaller banks begin to bid for o/n FF when reserves dwindle. Foreign banks under laxer regulation will also bid in o/n FF to lend dollars in other markets that offer wider (thus more profitable) spreads, such as when o/n GCF (interdealer repo rates) climb above the rate paid on their reserve balances, which they would otherwise park at the Fed.

All repo rates should, in theory, trade below interbank rates like o/n FF, as repos are secured loans and interbank loans are unsecured. Yet, Basel III creates distortions. Large U.S. banks, the former arbitrageurs, can no longer freely borrow in o/n FF, lend in repo, and “arb” spreads away without raising uneconomical levels of capital[20](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-20-173354458). In the Fed’s new system, o/n FF should instead trade undisturbed in a straight line, reflecting not only the ample levels of interbank liquidity but the subsequent rate at which FHLBs (unable to earn IORB) lend to foreign banks[21](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-reckoning-part-ii#footnote-21-173354458), the only class of bank that can profit from “IORB arbitrage” by borrowing reserves at [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) (at say 4.09%) and “parking” funds at the Fed to earn [IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB) (at say 4.15%).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!IUCS!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F96c400de-ca78-42d6-8096-0bd3eb24304d_4422x3078.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!IUCS!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F96c400de-ca78-42d6-8096-0bd3eb24304d_4422x3078.png)

the dips are foreign banks pulling back temporarily from arbitrage (IORB-EFFR) to window-dress their balance sheets on quarter-end dates

But these arbitrage trades have limits. FHLBs only have so many reserves to deploy while also being exposed to the same hierarchies as other market participants. Once FHLBs reach their tolerance levels, the likes of JPM must provide via their excess reserves. However, even JPM’s cushion will eventually fall to zero. That leaves market imbalances to be resolved at the apex of the hierarchy between dealers, banks, and the Fed via the SRF (and swap lines). The stigma created through central bank lending, however, persists. The Fed has been made aware directly, via [The Desk’s](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/market-intelligence) intelligence operations, that primary dealers will not comply without greater incentives.

If dealer banks won’t tap the SRF, that leaves the [G-SIBs](https://www.fsb.org/2024/11/2024-list-of-global-systemically-important-banks-g-sibs/) to settle imbalances before the Fed intervenes. Even so, banks aren’t other banks, hedge funds, or even the Fed’s best friend — they only make a market when it benefits their bottom line. When balance sheets grow too scarce and regulatory constraints grow binding, dealers’ priorities shift. The first “big switch” has been turned on in the dollar funding hierarchy: SOFR now trades above the rate paid to banks by the Fed, hence the major dollar clearers have the incentive to divert more liquidity away from the banking system and into repos for a tidier profit. Dollar market makers consequently have fewer bullets to deploy elsewhere, and not all the Big Six banks are equal actors in policing other upper limits of the Fed’s local and global jaws. Some don’t have excess reserves. Some don’t have “poachers” like JPM, and those poachers will possess less ammo over time.

The Fed’s QT persisting in an era of mammoth bill issuance makes it inevitable that all ultra-short-end rates must drift higher, eventually even o/n FF, soon exceeding IORB and then the SRF’s minimum bid. Hierarchies, once deactivated by large reserve balances and a chock-a-block RRP, have reactivated and returned money markets to a more volatile state, one in which the Fed will face greater challenges in predicting when to “land” dollar rates. If the U.S. central bank’s alarm system is effective, the ensuing takeoff and subsequent landing of money market rates should result in a smooth flight path. But linger in the air for too long, and the Fed’s course will start to grow turbulent.
