Remember, nothing in the piece is financial advice. If you act on anything, you agree to this [disclaimer](https://www.conks.plumbing/about#%C2%A7disclaimer). Thanks for being a Conks Subscriber! For more, check out [Pro](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/conks-pro).**_

# The Fed's New Target: Part I - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i
> rising interbank tensions will prompt the next rate evolution

---
_— click here for [Pro](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/pro-the-feds-new-target-part-one)_

Money markets have experienced [yet another](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-year-end-money-market-storm) quiet year-end. Just a week after ending its balance-sheet taper — where officials shrink the central bank balance sheet via QT[1](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-1-175057129) — the U.S. central bank has commenced an onslaught of reserve injections, marking the start of a _[Great Compression](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/plumbing-notes-the-great-compression)_ in money-market rates. Year-end will soon turn into a distant memory, with [repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) rates (SOFR) set to print well below the Fed’s new fixed [SRP](https://www.federalreserve.gov/monetarypolicy/standing-overnight-repurchase-agreements.htm) (Standing Repo) rate while emergency Fed [volumes](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/desk-operations/repo) top out. Large U.S. Treasury (UST) issuance combined with tighter liquidity was destined to make rate spikes a regular feature of the o/n (overnight) [rates complex](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!vIXN!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fab648550-95f4-45c7-b5aa-930e7466737e_4260x4044.png). Yet, the Fed’s [RMOs](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/plumbing-notes-its-not-qe) (reserve management operations) have thwarted any chance of a plumbing “hard landing”[2](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-2-175057129). The subsequent _Great Compression_ should not just contain rates within the U.S. central bank’s limits but enable officials to enforce a fully secured standard, no longer targeting overnight Fed Funds ([o/n FF](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-ff-aka-overnight-fed-funds)) but one of its numerous [repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) benchmarks. A target rate evolution is looming_._

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!fMJS!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7f8a231d-4135-462c-8fcf-20b36a308919_6300x6300.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!fMJS!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F7f8a231d-4135-462c-8fcf-20b36a308919_6300x6300.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop, available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-target-rate))

Just as the Fed’s new permanent open market operations ([POMOs](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/pomo_landing.html)) have commenced[3](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-3-175057129), a lengthy balance-sheet winter has concluded. Banks' balance sheets, upon the year-end “air pocket” in money markets[4](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-4-175057129), are now destined to expand rapidly, with expected plumbing relief priced in. The lengthy “tug of war” in [(swap) spreads](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!dtOp!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F5c4a12db-9b64-4af1-bd86-627012a087b6_2548x1393.png), _Conks’_ real-time gauge for measuring UST market frictions, has ended with an easing of constraints as the victor. This outcome was decided[5](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-5-175057129) in part via one of the most noteworthy[6](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-6-175057129) central bank leaders proposing a shift from targeting an unsecured rate ([o/n FF](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)) to targeting a “new-age” secured benchmark (repo) — a move intended to subdue volatility while granting the Fed even greater dominion over money markets[7](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-7-175057129).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!_LR7!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F67b4ec6e-a37f-4f19-aaca-e6076fef1675_3940x1932.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!_LR7!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F67b4ec6e-a37f-4f19-aaca-e6076fef1675_3940x1932.png)

Upon [delivering](https://www.dallasfed.org/news/speeches/logan/2025/lkl250925) a speech at the end of September last year, Dallas Fed President Logan[8](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-8-175057129) has initiated a shift toward the U.S. central bank replacing [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) — the benchmark for unsecured[9](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-9-175057129) interbank (i.e. o/n FF) rates[10](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-10-175057129) — with a repo benchmark as its “policy rate”, the Fed’s primary mechanism it uses to influence U.S. (hence global) financial and economic conditions. The [last](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-repo-market-dislocation-part-two) prominent evolution, the switch from [LIBOR](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Libor)[11](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-11-175057129) to SOFR[12](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-12-175057129), was completed just a few years ago, a decade after private and public-sector behemoths[13](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-13-175057129) sought to oust an unsecured standard, led by LIBOR, from the mainstream. Back then, a potential successor was whittled down to [OBFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/obfr)[14](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-14-175057129), a semi-global unsecured dollar rate, and [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr), a secured overnight ([repo](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market)) rate, with the latter crowned as the leading global benchmark for pricing trillions of dollars in financial assets. Yet, despite SOFR becoming LIBOR’s successor, monetary leaders still retained part of the old standard, preserving o/n FF ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)) as their desired target rate.

To this day, it has endured. But not for much longer. The near-death of the unsecured interbank market[15](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-15-175057129) has set the stage for monetary leaders to complete the shift away from targeting rates based on unsecured dealings. Central bank officials have grown wary of the frail linkages, primarily opportunistic [arbitrage trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-fed-funds-arbitrage), binding the Fed Funds market together.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop, available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-fed-funds-arbitrage))

Even so, rising uncertainty among major interbank players will be just one motivation for Fed alchemists to change targets. A fully secured standard awaits.

The Effective Fed Funds rate (EFFR), a relic and reincarnation[16](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-16-175057129) of a former dollar standard fueled largely by unsecured dollar trades, will soon be dethroned as the U.S. central bank’s target rate and consequently the target range. Although monetary leaders officially target one money market rate, they unofficially desire all their benchmark rates to print within a 25bps range set at each FOMC meeting. The U.S. central bank will thus soon prioritize steering repo rates over o/n FF ([EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr)). The Fed’s secured benchmarks, [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr), [TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr), and [BGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/bgcr) — which capture either most of the repo market or specific segments — have slowly taken over, with daily volumes exceeding $4.5 trillion. Rates on o/n FF trades[17](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-17-175057129), meanwhile**,** have collapsed to roughly $80-120 billion daily, while the closest measure to a global unsecured benchmark, [OBFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/obfr), has reached a maximum of [~$300 billion](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/obfr).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pWW-!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdb111817-c4ad-4e20-9c4c-956976652095_4246x3024.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pWW-!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fdb111817-c4ad-4e20-9c4c-956976652095_4246x3024.png)

labels above describe the most common funding chains

A sea of constraints imposed by the Fed, a.k.a its rendition of [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm), has obliterated the market for unsecured interbank (bank-to-bank) trades by forcing U.S. money dealers to raise — what were once — obscene amounts of capital to intermediate interbank markets. What’s left remains a fragile ecosystem, where volumes are dominated by obscure arbitrage in which a concentrated number of parties can pull back at any moment. The o/n FF market has almost entirely disconnected from its true purpose: resolving certain banks’ shortages of reserve balances used solely to settle payments. Without demand to borrow bank cash in the Fed’s [ample reserve regime](https://www.federalreserve.gov/econres/notes/feds-notes/implementing-monetary-policy-in-an-ample-reserves-regime-the-basics-note-1-of-3-20200701.html), large U.S. banks have retreated altogether. Most o/n FF volumes consequently arise from participants who have no desire to settle interbank payments, instead engaging in regulatory or pure money-market arbitrage. As one interbank trader described the o/n FF market, “it’s one big arb,” primarily between New York branches and agencies of foreign banks and Federal Home Loan Banks (FHLBs), the public lenders of _first_ resort.

Since [Basel III](https://www.bis.org/bcbs/basel3.htm) has “outlawed” other banks, and [Fannie and Freddie](https://www.fhfa.gov/about/fannie-mae-freddie-mac) have vacated the interbank realm, foreign banks serve as the dominant o/n FF borrower, and FHLBs serve as the only routine o/n FF lender. FHLBs deploy liquidity in o/n FF because they have no other way to offset the negative carry from “earning” zero in their Fed master accounts, as GSEs[18](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-18-175057129) (government-sponsored enterprises), such as FHLBs, can't earn interest on reserve balances ([IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB)) by law, unlike all other depository institutions.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop, available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-fed-funds-arbitrage))

On the opposite side of the trade, foreign banks (who earn IORB) borrow FHLBs’ non-interest-bearing reserves and park them in their Fed master accounts, earning the spread between the rate at which FHLBs lend to them in o/n FF and the U.S. central bank pays out via IORB. Only FBOs (foreign banking organizations) partake because, unlike U.S. banks, foreign bank branches don’t pay a lofty FDIC deposit insurance fee, thereby reducing the capital they need to earn the same spread as their U.S. rivals.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hbcS!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbfa6b729-d5a9-421d-a6e4-7a5c7ebd4ea6_4377x2535.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hbcS!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fbfa6b729-d5a9-421d-a6e4-7a5c7ebd4ea6_4377x2535.png)

foreign banks dominate the share of o/n FF borrowings

FHLBs also don’t have it easy. Failing to lend out reserves in the o/n FF market places a hefty tax on their liquidity portfolios[19](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-19-175057129), paying SOFR (say, 3.75%) on borrowings while “earning” zero on reserves. Idle balances must therefore be deployed.

Alas, for monetary leaders aiming to preserve o/n FF volumes, the Fed Funds market is not the only destination. FHLBs lend their reserves not just via o/n FF trades but also through repos and advances[20](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-20-175057129). FHLB trading desks can effortlessly redirect reserves into markets outside Fed Funds, but must fund these trades with reserve balances[21](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-21-175057129). This is just a taste of the potential trades that may pull further volumes out of the rate the Fed presently targets.

Worse still for Fed officials, FHLBs are not the sole interbank player possessing an ever-greater incentive to temporarily (or permanently) depart from the Fed Funds market.

Elsewhere, a market for [intraday megabank liquidity](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-market-for-intraday) has emerged between FHLBs and [G-SIBs](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/g-sibs), with the latter trying to meet a sea of liquidity requirements. Banks are now constrained not only by end-of-day but also by intraday liquidity and thus hoard [HQLA](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/hqla) (high-quality liquid assets), mostly reserves and USTs. Financial giants need to maintain adequate end-of-day liquidity (i.e. [HQLA](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/hqla)) by the close to report a compliant [LCR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/lcr-liquidity-coverage-ratio) ([liquidity coverage ratio](https://www.bis.org/publ/bcbs238.htm)), which mandates banks to cover potential outflows over a 30-day period of financial stress.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qdGt!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F79fcda66-15a0-4cb5-805c-3a4a1095805a_6237x4619.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!qdGt!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F79fcda66-15a0-4cb5-805c-3a4a1095805a_6237x4619.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop, available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-how-liquidity-requirements))

Intraday liquidity, however, will always supersede end-of-day liquidity as a bank’s most restrictive (a.k.a binding) liquidity constraint. Banking behemoths must also publish a “living will”, allowing the U.S. regulatory complex to resolve (i.e. wind down) large banks’ operations without relying on taxpayer money. Dubbed “resolution liquidity” by Fed architects, this requires banks to preserve HQLA throughout the day, _above and beyond_ expected outflows (highlighted in light blue above).

To maintain this intraday cushion, banks have started tapping markets outside of o/n FF, threatening target-rate volumes. Recent regulatory changes benefit interest-bearing deposit accounts, known as “IBDAs”, offered by FHLBs to megabanks. IBDAs remain superior for intraday liquidity needs by ensuring banks avoid sudden drops in reserves at any time during the day. These accounts provide “full 24/7 reserve cover”, unlike o/n FF trades, which must be renewed in the morning and involve reserves moving back and forth between FHLBs and U.S. G-SIBs’ Fed master accounts. These tend to “trade” way above o/n FF — and sometimes outside the Fed’s target range! Until recently, o/n FF trades were the only instruments that regulators excluded from FHLB’s counterparty lending limits when extending unsecured credit to a single counterparty. But in January ‘25, the FHFA[22](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-22-175057129) [made](https://www.fhfa.gov/news/news-release/fhfa-announces-final-rule-expanding-access-to-liquidity-for-the-federal-home-loan-bank-system) IBDAs “fungible” with o/n FF trades by applying the same exemption, attempting to remove the hefty spread between IBDA and o/n FF rates and reduce interbank frictions.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hkTx!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89202213-51eb-46a5-908f-e2f87cb7f946_4324x2499.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hkTx!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F89202213-51eb-46a5-908f-e2f87cb7f946_4324x2499.png)

Fast forward to today: despite regulators’ efforts, IBDA volumes have yet to expand significantly. The market for intraday megabank liquidity is no match for IORB arbitrage, the latter of which can generate at least $80 billion in volume whenever foreign banks are game. Still, IBDA usage remains a future threat to the Fed’s target range, if “unsecured”[23](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-23-175057129).

The threats to o/n FF volumes don’t stop at intraday interbank markets: they extend to intraday “shadow banking”[24](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-24-175057129). A competing intraday repo market has emerged, conceived by the BNY (Bank of New York). “Intraday triparty” allows market participants to lend and borrow cash for as little as one hour during the day, rather than the usual overnight period. These repos have been touted as a game-changer for intraday liquidity, yet this market has failed to gain much traction. The BNY’s other new service, however, dubbed “early return triparty”, returns cash in the morning. This allows FHLBs to preserve their morning cash buffers mandated by their regulator, creating another potential drag on o/n FF volumes that the Fed continues to target.

As an increasing number of obscure, emerging market segments compete with o/n FF for volumes, it’s only going to get worse for the U.S. central bank from here on out. Not to mention, the public lenders of last resort — FHLBs — not only overlook but also retreat from the interbank market in times of crisis, when they are traditionally meant to backstop o/n FF volumes the most.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9f7c!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F64ba1e49-8f03-4e0e-b8d2-821fb41efb0b_4383x2800.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!9f7c!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F64ba1e49-8f03-4e0e-b8d2-821fb41efb0b_4383x2800.png)

If the Fed doesn’t modernize soon, it will continue to target a rate at which almost all associated trades are not only unsecured but also driven by participants who tend to retreat rather than fuel a deep, liquid market. Interbank relationships will remain concentrated — not diversified — with greater odds that major interbank players will pull back at the worst possible moment. The resulting lower volumes in the Fed Funds market increase the danger of o/n FF spiking and destabilizing the Fed’s present — yet, in many ways, legacy — target rate. o/n FF is akin to a micro-cap stock, with the likelihood that large volume disappears from the market increasing as each financial quarter passes. Subsequently, the overnight _unsecured_ interbank market has become a pure opportunistic arena, where rates are held in limbo by a cluster of obscure arbitrage trades, each more intricate than the last.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!pNbb!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3ba74052-8d7d-4365-80ad-89ba6b19f594_6900x6300.png)

same infographic as above, added again here for convenience

Unsurprisingly, the prominent interbank activity and primary stabilizer of the Fed’s target rate no longer involves trades between large U.S. banks and their smaller counterparts. In the pre-GFC era, large money center giants regularly fell short of reserves, so borrowed from other giants and large regional banks to end the day with their Fed accounts in the green. But since the passing of Lehman Brothers, unsecured interbank volumes have fallen by approximately 70%. Arbitrage between foreign banks and FHLBs now makes up over 90% of o/n FF trades.

At the crack of dawn, FHLBs either lend their spare reserve balances or raise a few billion by issuing discount notes (DNs), typically with an overnight tenor via the “[FHLB Window](https://www.fhlb-of.com/ofweb_userWeb/pageBuilder/window-rates-42),” bought by the largest broker-dealers to distribute among their clients. Thus, depending on the funding method, FHLBs earn o/n FF or the spread between o/n FF and o/n DN. The public lenders of _first_ resort lend this cash (i.e. reserve balances) to their regular foreign bank counterparties or other arbitrageurs, such as credit unions, via Fed Funds brokers in New York.

Foreign banks then “park” or “deploy” the reserves they receive, parking them in their Fed master accounts to earn IORB or lending them out whenever spreads between o/n FF and other money markets widen. During the Fed’s latest balance-sheet taper, FBOs deployed a greater number of their excess reserves into higher-yielding repos and FX swaps. In both scenarios, their U.S rivals didn’t stand much chance of contesting, crowded out by either FDIC deposit fees or daily averaging. Unlike their U.S. rivals, foreign banks don’t pay deposit fees, while some only report balance sheet[25](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-25-175057129) on month-ends. For U.S. banks to find IORB arbitrage attractive, [IORB-EFFR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/iorb-effr) must widen by ~400% intraday, and even so, they’re still crowded out by their foreign rivals.

A sharp plumber can detect when foreign banks dominate interbank activity by observing the signature quarter-end “dips” in o/n FF volumes, where foreign banks retreat from arbitrage to “window-dress”: enhancing their balance-sheet metrics before reporting to the U.S. regulatory complex.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!q9pA!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9c53572a-d0ab-45e0-9ba0-f4d71a689a17_4464x3107.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!q9pA!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9c53572a-d0ab-45e0-9ba0-f4d71a689a17_4464x3107.png)

Even with foreign banks pulling back, however, o/n FF volume persists, revealing other ongoing arbitrage trades. Once IORB arbitrage evaporates, the remaining interbank “plays” are unveiled. Yet again, these trades barely capture the rates at which banks boost their cash balances to rectify genuine reserve shortages. The U.S. interbank market also operates as a “deli” for other niche trades, with FHLBs once again the store owner and commercial banks the customers. If banks aren’t retreating, they’re exploiting, another motive for the U.S. central bank to ditch its unsecured target rate.

Perhaps the most infamous scenario involves medium-sized banks — legally — gaming their regulatory ratios. Large regional U.S. banks, plus a small number of [FBOs](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/fbos-foreign-banking-organizations) under the Fed’s all-seeing eye, tap the o/n FF market to boost their liquidity scores, a.k.a their LCRs. This trade only works when borrowing from FHLBs, which regulators deem lower risk from a liquidity perspective than borrowing from other interbank players. When raising bank cash from JPM, o/n FF borrowers must hold 100% HQLA (i.e. a 1:1 backing), compared to a 40% backing when borrowing from FHLBs. Not only that, but lending unsecured to other banks increases major banks’ [G-SIB scores](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/g-sib-surcharge) and requires them to raise additional capital and issue term debt. Accordingly, at all costs, U.S. banks avoid lending in o/n FF, while raising cash elsewhere, usually via more balance-sheet-friendly (hence cheaper) o/n repos. Nevertheless, as long as FHLBs remain willing to serve as Fed Funds lenders, however, regulatory arbitrage shall prevail. In what _Conks_ calls the “LCR booster” trade, [smaller banks](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-fed-funds-liquidity)[26](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-26-175057129) turn up just before reporting on month-ends to bid for reserves, solely from FHLBs, to boost their LCRs and appear healthier to regulators.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-iE6!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Feb8f1dc5-5479-422f-9fbc-18db8faf4d17_6900x6900.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!-iE6!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Feb8f1dc5-5479-422f-9fbc-18db8faf4d17_6900x6900.png)

click to enlarge (best viewed on desktop, available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-fed-funds-liquidity))

Any bank deemed “[Category IV](https://www.federalreserve.gov/aboutthefed/boardmeetings/files/tailoring-rule-visual-20191010.pdf)”[27](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-27-175057129), a number of large regional banks and smaller U.S. branches of foreign banks, can still boost their LCRs on month-ends, generating billions in o/n FF volumes. In fact, multiple o/n FF dealings stem from the need not just to boost but also to satisfy LCRs, to such an extent that the Fed has dubbed smaller players “LCR banks”.

Though LCR banks generate activity, they play a relatively small role in generating o/n FF volumes. The major volume producer is, of course, foreign banks.

Recently, however, they have been pulling back.

FHLBs have increased the cost of o/n FF liquidity by jacking up interbank rates, narrowing the IORB-EFFR spread. They have begun shifting from funding IORB arbitrage to funding advances and repos after their bargaining power has increased. Foreign banks now earn just a 1bps spread ([IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB)\-[EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) = 1bps) to provide volumes for the Fed to target. Likely aiding their recent decision to stop shredding reserves, monetary leaders want FHLBs to fuel more IORB arbitrage trades via suppressing o/n FF. The TGA (the U.S. government’s bank account held at the Fed) keeps sucking an increasing number of reserves from the system. RMOs will offset that slightly but will only provide a buffer, not an abundance of liquidity. As _Conks_ previously warned, reserves are no longer plentiful, and the Fed is not planning to flood interbank markets, instead keeping levels ticking over.

As reserves hover slightly above ample, a once-idle o/n FF will grow more volatile, oscillating just below IORB. Once a nothingburger with trillions in excess reserves, lower o/n FF volumes now cause EFFR to print unsteady. The age of a stable o/n FF rate is over.

The tiny number of transactions that fuel genuine demand for settling interbank payments tend to trade at a premium above IORB rather than at the average unsecured interbank rate. If that’s not a glaring signpost for the U.S. central bank to begin switching targets, nothing else will be. EFFR, the king of unsecured interbank markets, is more than outdated. It’s about time officials settled on a target rate successor.

With its major stabilizing force becoming increasingly erratic, EFFR, as a target rate among a myriad of potential alternatives, no longer has any major advantage. The last remaining benefit is that o/n FF will continue to be the last benchmark that prints outside the Fed’s [target range](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/target-band) during stormy periods. But that hardly justifies targeting a rate based primarily on non-U.S. banks engaging in arbitrage.

Luckily, with Fed Logan and, consequently, other central bank leaders’ endorsement, a switch to a new target should at least capture trades in the secured rate arena, where markets are deep and liquid, plus funding costs reflect actual borrowing costs, not opportunistic transactions. In her September speech, the Dallas Fed leader proposed using [TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr) (the Triparty General Collateral Rate), the Fed’s benchmark for secured cash lending at the “base layer” of repo: [the triparty market](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market) _(highlighted in yellow below)_. This primarily captures the cost of the Fed’s primary dealers and other large dealers funding their operations[28](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i#footnote-28-175057129).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ooWF!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F827b3a3b-8f37-4ec3-8257-6fbe3a0cc9c5_7863x6663.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ooWF!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F827b3a3b-8f37-4ec3-8257-6fbe3a0cc9c5_7863x6663.png)

note: the SRF is now known as an SRP (Standing Repo) operation

The U.S. central bank’s first task will thus be to transition from an unsecured to secured target rate, from targeting trades based on arbitrage to those based on dealer banks’ funding costs. Following that switch, the sky’s the limit for the Fed’s monetary alchemists. Next stop: SOFR or another creation.

As of September ‘25, Fed Logan, possessing the unofficial title of top target-rate influencer, has ignited a slow-burning fuse inside U.S. central bank circles. A shift to targeting a secured Fed benchmark is likely assured. Whether TGCR, SOFR, or a new benchmark serves as the new target rate will be a major money market enigma over the coming quarters...

_**...the “roadmap” and outcomes of which will be unveiled in Part II.**_

# Plumbing Notes: Repo 101 - Conks
+ author: Conks
+ source: https://www.conks.plumbing/p/plumbing-notes-repo-101
> a concise primer on repo market lingo & mechanics

---
Recently, we published a “mega-infographic” that unveiled the plumbing of the repo market in detail.

Given the amount of information required to demonstrate the complete flows of a repo trade, the graphic is tough to navigate without a PDF version. Subsequently, we’re providing one for all readers below.

Still, even with complete visuals, a written explanation is needed to comprehend such an intricate ecosystem. Welcome to a Repo 101: a brief primer that will complement _Part II_ of our current [target rate series](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i), covering repo lingo and mechanics in plain English. This is not the usual _Conks_ style of article. Instead, it’s written to clarify all key repo fundamentals and rates as concisely as possible.

If you’re an active money market participant or keen observer, you’ll soon discover why jargon in repo markets is unfortunately essential. For instance, “o/n TPR” (pronounced in English as “overnight triparty” — the meaning of which we’ll get to further on) is much easier to say than “the average overnight rate on non-centrally cleared triparty general collateral repos”!

Fortunately, over time, market participants have condensed various elements of the repo ecosystem into a succinct language. Learning this lingo is necessary to avoid going insane, but once acquired, understanding repo markets becomes straightforward. And the flows in the infographic above gain purpose and meaning.

For the uninitiated, a repo (short for repurchase agreement) is a cash loan secured against collateral. The cash borrower sells the security and repurchases it at a higher price at a later date. The difference in price, which is converted into a standardized repo rate, is the interest earned and the cost of borrowing cash. Trades are known as a reverse repo (RRP) from the perspective of the cash lender, and a repo from the perspective of the cash borrower, so reverse repos and repos are part of the same transaction, but the terminology is used to define which side of the repo trade someone is on. Even so, everybody calls it the repo market — not the RRP (reverse repo) market. In the opening “leg”, cash and collateral swap hands. In the closing leg, which occurs after the agreed loan period (such as o/n — overnight), cash and collateral are returned to their original owners, along with any repo interest owed by the cash borrower to the cash lender. Voila! A repo transaction.

Two types of repo exist: GC (general collateral) and SC (specific collateral) repos.

General collateral (GC) repos get their name from the fact that the end objective is either to raise cash (from the cash borrower’s perspective) or to earn interest (from the cash lender’s stance). As long as the collateral is sourced from a basket of high-quality securities, such as U.S. Treasuries and Agency MBS, the cash lender doesn’t care what backs the loan. If they can earn yield securely, then “general”, a.k.a. a broad range of securities, suffices. The Bank of New York (BNY) operates the “triparty” platform, the only venue for GC repo trading. “Triparty” and “GC” are therefore used interchangeably or concurrently to describe both the triparty repo market and GC repos. For instance, “triparty”, “triparty GC“, or “triparty general collateral” all refer to the triparty platform where GC repos trade exclusively.

In a GC repo, the cash borrower doesn’t want to trade with the cash it receives (as accounts on the triparty repo platform are for raising cash balances, not for active trading of securities; dealers have other accounts for that outside triparty), while the cash lender only wants to earn interest and doesn’t want to trade the security pledged to them in secondary markets. Thus, cash and collateral are locked in “boxes” on the triparty system (a.k.a “on the books” of a triparty custodian) to provide extra security for both parties. What’s more, since the lender doesn’t care (within reason) what security backs the loan, the BNY’s triparty system allocates a random security (hence general collateral) from the cash borrower’s account to back the loan. Ultimately, GC, a.k.a triparty, repos enable seamless, secure cash lending and borrowing. Dealers keep their overall cash levels in the green, and money market funds earn interest.

In contrast, specific collateral (SC) repos get their name from the fact that the main goal for the cash lender (also a securities borrower) is to obtain specific assets in their trading accounts, which they will use to enter trades outside repo markets, such as in the UST cash market (a.k.a. the secondary Treasury market). Unlike GC repos, cash and securities can be accessed and moved between participants during the opening and closing legs. The vast majority of these repos finance [relative-value and basis trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-bilateral-repo-markets) in U.S. Treasury markets that require specific securities (hence, specific collateral) to execute, e.g. delivering a bond into a futures contract.

To facilitate these trades, [Fedwire](https://www.frbservices.org/financial-services/securities) — the Fed’s settlement system — is (almost) always the venue for SC repos. DVP (delivery vs. payment) is the settlement mechanism used by Fedwire to securely transfer cash and securities between accounts. Accordingly, like with “triparty” and “GC”, “SC repos” are also called “[DVP repos](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-bilateral-repo-markets)”. Another term for SC repos is “bilateral” repos, as there’s no third-party custody of cash and securities during trades. To reiterate, “DVP”, “bilateral”, and SC repos are the same thing. Participants, however, overwhelmingly refer to SC repo markets as “DVP repo”.

One type of repo (GC) tends to finance the other type (SC), as GC is a pure funding vehicle and SC is a trading vehicle. The major hurdle is gaining access to the markets required to execute elaborate fixed-income [trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-bilateral-repo-markets?utm_source=publication-search), which now hold the fixed-income ecosystem together.

Repo markets are fragmented. How trades are cleared and settled determines where certain types of participants trade. Each type (GC and SC) has its own market for “centrally cleared” and “non-centrally cleared” dealings — which participants shorten to “cleared” and “uncleared” markets for simplicity. Central clearing providers (a.k.a. central counterparties or CCPs) provide dealers, brokers, and banks with risk management, such as absorbing losses from a counterparty default, in exchange for fees and the posting of margin. Membership is required, costly, and granted only after meeting strict criteria, usually met by large banks, brokers, or dealers. Consequently, only major banks, dealers, and other large players are likely to become members of a clearinghouse, and cleared markets (to reiterate, this means centrally cleared; all trades are cleared in some capacity) are classed as interdealer markets — and even interbank markets, as all large dealers tend to be subsidiaries of banks (known as [dealer banks](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/dealer-banks) for short). All trades that go through central clearing — hence interdealer markets — will pass through the accounts of the [FICC](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov?utm_source=google&utm_medium=cpc&utm_campaign=sifmu_css_gsd_eg&gad_source=1&gad_campaignid=20418455415&gbraid=0AAAAAD-f9kWRZRg4Y0Kv_sBxV-V6jyr2C&gclid=Cj0KCQiA-NHLBhDSARIsAIhe9X3e63swJewBJIOxRl_5U2Zg_JCi8ZprFV8YtuPS7OwGyiTsfiAtb8caAkwhEALw_wcB) (Fixed Income Clearing Corporation), as it’s the sole central clearing provider in U.S. repo markets.

To trade GC (general collateral) repos, participants must have access to BNY’s triparty repo platform and be established firms. Money market funds are the major cash lenders, and cash borrowers are the largest dealers that money market funds are willing to lend to. The uncleared triparty segment, simply “triparty”, is “open to all”, as trades aren’t centrally cleared by the FICC. Alongside the main triparty market, though, is GCF (General Collateral Financing) Repo, the interdealer segment of triparty, in which trades are centrally cleared via FICC’s GCF Repo service. Dealers consequently trade GC repos with money funds in triparty and with fellow dealers and banks in GCF Repo. A great divide.

As for trading SC (specific collateral) repos, participants must have access to either the FICC’s DVP Repo service — the interdealer market for SC repos, or “NCCBR” (non-centrally cleared bilateral repo), a name that encompasses all the aforementioned jargon. This remains the uncleared market for SC repos. Similar to triparty and GCF Repo, dealers and banks trade in DVP repo, and dealers and hedge funds trade in NCCBR. Money funds are barely active, since they prefer tri-party repos, specifically designed for “basic” cash lending.

A repo market liberation, however, has gotten underway. In both GC and SC segments, members of the FICC (dealers and banks) can more easily sponsor or act as agents for their clients, who do not hold an FICC membership, to trade in centrally cleared markets. These services, all created by the FICC, are called [Sponsored Repo](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) (Sponsored GC and Sponsored DVP) and the [Agent Clearing Service](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/agent-clearing-service) (DVP ACS and Triparty ACS). A growing number of repo segments and services now exist to support the evolution toward most trades being centrally cleared, thereby reducing systemic risk. That leaves NCCBR to eventually collapse in volume, as traders depart for cleared DVP markets, NCCBR’s centrally cleared rivals.

Out of the chaos, four key rates have arisen: o/n TPR, o/n GC(F), o/n DVP, and o/n NCCBR. Repos trade across multiple timeframes and are collateralized by various types of securities, yet the common practice is to publish and track rates based on overnight (o/n) trades backed by U.S. Treasuries — as the Fed does with its repo benchmarks ([TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr), [BGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/bgcr), and [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr)).

o/n TPR (overnight triparty) refers to o/n rates on triparty repos a.k.a uncleared GC repos. In English, o/n TPR measures the average overnight rate at which dealers finance their operations from money funds. There are many other lenders and borrowers in triparty, but these two parties are the major rate-setters. o/n TPR is observable via the Fed’s [TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr) (triparty general collateral rate) benchmark. Meanwhile, o/n GC refers to the average rate in the interdealer triparty market (the FICC’s GCF Repo Service), where dealers and banks lend and borrow cash among themselves. o/n GC is observable via the DTCC’s GCF Repo Index [benchmark](https://www.dtcc.com/charts/dtcc-gcf-repo-index).

Meanwhile, in SC markets, o/n DVP refers to the average rate in DVP Repo, the interdealer DVP market (the FICC’s DVP Repo Service), where dealers and banks source securities among themselves. It also includes rates in [Sponsored DVP](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) and DVP [ACS](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/agent-clearing-service). o/n DVP is observable via the OFR’s Cleared Repo [benchmark](https://www.financialresearch.gov/short-term-funding-monitor/datasets/repo-single/?mnemonic=REPO-DVP_AR_OO-F).

Finally, o/n NCCBR refers to the average overnight rate on non-centrally cleared bilateral repos. In English, o/n NCCBR measures rates at which dealers lend to hedge funds without going through central clearing. Unfortunately, the obscure nature of this segment means no published rate is available to track, but it likely trades somewhere between o/n DVP and o/n GC.

Adding context to these rates are the flows among participants on the “bid side” (i.e. those raising cash by pledging securities) and “offer side” (i.e. those lending cash secured against collateral). In one chain, dealers borrow cash from money funds at o/n TPR and lend it to other dealers in o/n GC or o/n DVP, or to hedge funds in o/n NCCBR. In a more specific example, a dealer who has access to cleared markets sponsors a hedge fund to trade in Sponsored DVP, allowing the hedge fund to go long and short two similar Treasuries on leverage in a relative-value trade.

As is evident, the repo market’s intricacy has created an abundance of jargon, but it’s justified to stop participants — and _Conks —_ from going insane. Hopefully, learning the lingo above will help you, too, to navigate money markets with less friction. Armed with this primer and our mega-infographic, you should now be able to analyze repo markets with confidence, knowing what’s occurring beneath an obscure exterior.

# (2) The Fed's New Target: Part II - Conks
+ author: Substack
+ source: https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587
> the secured rate complex and the Fed’s new target

---
_— click here for [Part I](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i) and here for [Pro](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/pro-the-feds-new-target-part-ii)_

With a _[Great Compression](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/plumbing-notes-the-great-compression)_ in money market rates underway, it’s the ideal moment for Fed officials to switch to a new target rate. As _[Part I](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i)_ unveiled in detail, volume in overnight Fed Funds (o/n FF) — the average rate of which the Fed currently targets to influence economic and financial conditions — has collapsed. Remaining o/n FF activities also fail to reflect the actual cost of tapping U.S. dollars while remaining vulnerable to a cluster of emerging rival interbank markets. The mechanism underlying the U.S. central bank’s primary monetary tool is thus approaching its breaking point.

For decades, targeting an unsecured interbank rate, such as o/n FF, made total sense. The Fed Funds market thrived and reflected the genuine price at which banks financed their operations. That ended, however, in 2008, with Lehman Brothers’ sudden demise. Unsecured interbank volumes soon fell by ~80%, while reserves injected by Fed officials ballooned by multiple trillions. The latter has served as a stabilizing force for [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr) (i.e. o/n FF), enabling officials to avoid updating the target rate for more than a decade. Regardless, instability has risen sufficiently to warrant the next target-rate evolution.

To supplant o/n FF, central bank alchemists will explore multiple configurations: targeting an administered rate (e.g., [IORB](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/IORB) or [o/n RRP](https://fred.stlouisfed.org/series/RRPONTSYAWARD)), targeting an existing benchmark, assembling a “rates basket” and targeting its average, or constructing and targeting a new benchmark altogether. The ideal successor, though, grows evident quickly.

Aiming o/n rates at one of its administered rates — i.e. preset rates that the Fed charges or pays, such as IORB (the rate the Fed pays on banks’ reserve balances), will receive little backing. On top of breaking or disrupting the many mechanisms that maintain a stable [o/n rate complex](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ExWh!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F3b2399fa-4b5f-4f85-9384-44d841acbfdb_4260x4044.png), targeting an administered rate will spark a power shift within Fed circles. The Board of Governors determines IORB, the FOMC (comprising the same Fed Governors) sets the central bank rate on “emergency” o/n repos ([o/n SRP](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-srps-aka-overnight-fed-repos) or [SRPR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/srpr)) and reverse repos (o/n RRP), plus the board at each regional Reserve Bank (upon review by Fed Governors) establishes the [Discount Window](https://www.frbdiscountwindow.org/) rate. Switching to an administrative rate target would consequently transfer power from one faction to another, granting specific individuals greater influence over central bank policy while potentially eroding public trust and confidence.

As with incoming Fed Chair Warsh's [plan](https://www.ft.com/content/e29721dc-2a70-42b8-9fdc-c1e93792c359) to shrink the central bank balance sheet, the odds that an administered rate will serve as the new target rate are virtually zero. The same goes for targeting the average of a basket of o/n rates, adding complexity to an already elaborate process. Among other drawbacks, different Federal Reserve governors would start to favor certain rates within the basket, a recipe for disaster when communicating with the market and the public, and when reaching consensus among voting members.

Instead, minus creating a new benchmark, one remaining format stands out as the path of least resistance for the Fed. The U.S. central bank will almost certainly opt to use an existing secured benchmark to supersede [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr), one of two unsecured benchmarks. Its obscure companion, the Overnight Bank Funding Rate ([OBFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/obfr)), will likewise fail to serve as the Fed’s new “archery board”, as it measures rates on a portion of o/n FF trades plus trades from other unsecured markets generating limited volumes. No secured rates in sight.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Rmmj!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9d9adf18-6ecb-4aa8-aa0e-e539b0669e6b_4246x3024.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Rmmj!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F9d9adf18-6ecb-4aa8-aa0e-e539b0669e6b_4246x3024.png)

OBFR (dark blue) adds barely any extra volumes over o/n FF (teal)

That leaves an existing or new secured-rate benchmark as the only viable EFFR substitute. Officials must implement a target rate that transmits monetary policy efficiently and tracks underlying markets in which both sides of the order book have strong incentives to show up day after day. Conveniently, the Fed’s nascent secured rate benchmarks[1](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-1-186621587) combined have grown to become the Jupiter of o/n money markets: large, active, and capturing rates across almost[2](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-2-186621587) every repo market segment. Their gravity is overwhelming. If SOFR, TGCR, and BGCR move, you know money market rates have shifted. Repo rates have thus become more sensitive to changes in reserve levels than rates tracking the underlying market for interbank liquidity.

Before the Fed flooded the system with reserves[3](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-3-186621587), o/n FF functioned as an ideal benchmark. Without a wall of reserves held to protect against 30 days of outflows, a.k.a [the Fed’s LCR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/lcr-liquidity-coverage-ratio), to fall back on, numerous banks had to borrow in o/n FF each day. Thus, unsecured interbank rates closely tracked the cost of $ funding. But, as _[Part I](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-feds-new-target-part-i?open=false#%C2%A7the-interbank-market-arbitrage)_ also revealed, monetary leaders have slowly but surely killed the Fed Funds market's original purpose. Having built a war chest of reserves to weather an entire month of turmoil, banks have shifted to making markets for dollars on a secured basis, primarily in repo[4](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-4-186621587). Their dealer subsidiaries take in dollars from money funds (MMFs)[5](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-5-186621587) and lend mainly to speculative funds, whose leveraged trades supercharge liquidity in sovereign debt and other fixed-income markets.

The secured rate complex now reigns supreme. Switching to a secured rate _target_ will nonetheless be anything but straightforward and may cause a ruckus in various funding markets. Rates traders beware.

Traders must remain wary of a particular “white swan”[6](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-6-186621587) in funding markets. Implementing a new target rate will be far from a speedy process, spanning three quarters to two years from selection to execution. Yet, markets will be ready to respond to any incoming details, immediately pricing in the likely effects of whatever “divine” benchmark the Fed chooses. What’s more, each potential target will move markets differently.

To unveil potential scenarios, _Conks_ has devised — alongside a [concise primer](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/plumbing-notes-repo-101) — an extensive map of U.S. repo markets, with major segments and their flows color-coded. Each highlights numerous repo rates, from which numerous combinations form the Fed’s secured benchmarks: [TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr), [BGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/bgcr), and [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr). Before delving deeper into any central bank benchmark, however, it’s crucial to dissect the assortment of secured rates and flows.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZtKr!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd88135e8-8463-4ddc-a608-70d97b9e218a_6151x6250.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!ZtKr!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd88135e8-8463-4ddc-a608-70d97b9e218a_6151x6250.png)

image version of the above PDF (available in [light mode](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-modern-repo-market))

From right to left, the map exhibits each stage of a trade for every repo segment, from market access to [clearing](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/clearing) to [settlement](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/settlement) to end objectives – e.g. [RV funds trading USTs on leverage](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-relative-value-trading). Grey dotted lines indicate how market participants gain access to each segment, determined by class, size, capability, and power. Once admission is granted, colored dotted lines link participants to their desired repo segment. Specific players have different goals, but the “public good” of repo markets is to finance leveraged trades that enhance price efficiency and liquidity in U.S. fixed-income markets, mainly in U.S. [sovereign](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/sovereign-debt) and [agency debt](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/agency-debt). The subsequent funding chains produce a highly liquid but highly levered sovereign-debt machine[7](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-7-186621587).

Soon, though, obtaining and providing leverage will grow more difficult. The U.S. SEC (The Securities and Exchange Commission) has imposed mandatory [central clearing](https://www.sec.gov/featured-topics/treasury-clearing-implementation) — where a [central counterparty](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/central-counterparty-aka-ccpcentral-clearing-provider) (a.k.a CCP) stands between buyers and sellers to reduce systemic risk — for [almost](https://firstderivative.com/insights/us-treasury-central-clearing-a-rule-summary/#:~:text=Exclusions%20to%20the%20scope) all U.S. Treasury-backed repos conducted with members[8](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-8-186621587) of a CCP — e.g. primary dealers and banks — by July 2027.

The majority of volumes must, as of writing, pass through the [FICC](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov?utm_source=google&utm_medium=cpc&utm_campaign=sifmu_css_gsd_eg&gad_source=1&gad_campaignid=20418455415&gbraid=0AAAAAD-f9kVuiAM3Pw6W9PWVTQhf7hOIa&gclid=Cj0KCQiAkPzLBhD4ARIsAGfah8gE-uIjmPx1yiOQcLh-w0tQod0mjGOHeoiSADwCUdyFuXurIaoGPwAaAvNyEALw_wcB) (Fixed Income Clearing Corporation), the sole provider of centrally cleared trading in U.S. repo markets. Most non-centrally cleared (a.k.a “uncleared”) trades[9](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-9-186621587), which provide higher leverage via less regulatory oversight, will soon be outlawed. More segments have thus emerged to enable central clearing for most participants, prompting a [slow exodus](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/the-repo-market-exodus-part-i) from the uncleared provinces of repo[10](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-10-186621587) to an ever-expanding “alphabet soup” of centrally-cleared regions[11](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-11-186621587).

For major cash lenders (and thus major financiers of leverage) to comply with the SEC, they must rotate out of the triparty _uncleared_ market — where [money funds](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/money-funds), [securities lenders](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/securities-lenders), and other large institutions lend dollars to large dealers and banks securely to earn interest[12](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-12-186621587) — into triparty _cleared_ markets[13](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-13-186621587) — the same market but with the FICC providing central clearing. Rotating into cleared markets should have increased costs and frictions, yet alongside its longstanding [Sponsored (Triparty) GC](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) service, the FICC has devised numerous segments[14](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-14-186621587) that reduce both margin and capital requirements. The above map’s yellow flows demonstrate, post the July ‘26 deadline, how money funds and other cash lenders will continue to operate to acquire a secure yield: lending to well-capitalized, creditworthy borrowers, all of which will be FICC members or their clients granted access[15](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-15-186621587). The liquidity in triparty will become unrivaled.

Meanwhile, it's business as usual in GCF (General Collateral Financing) Repo, the triparty market’s interdealer segment (highlighted in green on the map). All trades are centrally cleared, so no action is required. The FICC’s GCF Service is open only to its members: largely [dealer banks](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/dealer-banks), plus their brokers. Thus, it is also deemed an interbank market. Rates on GCF repos trade at higher rates than regular (a.k.a uncleared) triparty trades, reflecting the higher cost of obtaining liquidity. Smaller dealers with FICC membership may be too risky to earn the business of some money funds active in the regular triparty markets, whether centrally cleared or not.

Triparty[16](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-16-186621587) markets may offer deep liquidity, but they don’t provide access to securities, preventing hedge funds from executing key leveraged trades. “Bilateral” repo markets[17](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-17-186621587) are the only markets that serve that purpose, collectively known as “DVP”[18](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-18-186621587), involving the transfer of cash and collateral between trading accounts, unlike in triparty. The cash lender, more of a securities borrower, uses securities funded via DVP repos to enter leveraged trades in the [secondary cash markets](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-us-treasury-cash), such as the U.S. Treasury market.

The DVP Repo Service (highlighted in blue) offered by FICC serves as the interdealer segment, thus the centrally cleared DVP market, alongside [Sponsored DVP](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) and [DVP ACS](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/agent-clearing-service) for non-FICC members. NCCBR, non-centrally cleared bilateral repo (highlighted in purple), meanwhile, is the market for uncleared DVP repos and — possessing the largest uncleared volumes – will undergo the largest exodus when central clearing becomes mandatory.

These segments converge to form a set of o/n rates[19](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-19-186621587) that cash lenders receive, cash borrowers pay, and dealers earn as they compete with one another to connect both sides of the trade. This set also forms various spreads[20](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-20-186621587) at which dealers make markets — a.k.a their profit. Rates and spreads continue to rise further up the typical hierarchy of triparty, GCF, DVP, and NCCBR. As long as a dealer believes it can charge a positive market-making spread — i.e. the cost of its cash borrowing (repos) remains cheaper than the gain on its cash lending (RRPs) – it will intermediate between any two segments. The following graphic provides an overview of o/n rates and spreads (↕) earned by dealers and other market makers during their operations in the secured rate complex...

...and the following infographics reveal, again using participants’ balance sheets, the trades that shape each secured benchmark[21](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-21-186621587).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!sAT5!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd3e3121c-305d-4cfa-a88c-15b3fc658c86_3950x932.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!sAT5!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd3e3121c-305d-4cfa-a88c-15b3fc658c86_3950x932.png)

In the uncleared [triparty market](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market), the “base layer” of the secured rate complex, repos trade at o/n TPR. Dealers take the cash from money funds raised at the regular uncleared triparty market rate, denoted as o/n TPR(unc)[22](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-22-186621587), and lend it into one of several segments, provided it yields a positive market-making spread.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Te0h!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Feed43696-ae8d-48c0-bf3e-c9fe8892f719_3959x1498.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Te0h!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Feed43696-ae8d-48c0-bf3e-c9fe8892f719_3959x1498.png)

notes: unc = uncleared, BNY = Bank of New York

The narrowest involves lending cash in GCF Repo (highlighted in green) to other dealers and banks. At a wider spread, dealers lend cash to other dealers and banks in DVP Repo and to hedge funds in Sponsored DVP and ACS (highlighted in blue). The largest spread, excluding lending into other dollar funding markets, can be earned by lending cash on a non-centrally cleared basis in NCCBR (highlighted in purple).

Large dealers and other FICC members also grant money funds and hedge fund clients access to cleared repo transactions in the growing number of cleared triparty segments, trading, on average, at the higher o/n TPR(c)[23](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-23-186621587).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!DbgF!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe5d026be-ba71-4e0a-ae00-206027888b55_3923x1306.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!DbgF!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fe5d026be-ba71-4e0a-ae00-206027888b55_3923x1306.png)

notes: c = cleared, BNY = Bank of New York

Dealers with FICC membership can also raise cash from fellow members in GCF Repo, like primary dealers with excess cash lending to smaller dealers without access to the uncleared triparty market[24](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-24-186621587). Dealers can also tap dollars from GCF to lend into most other segments, such as borrowing at o/n GCF and lending into o/n DVP or o/n TPR(c), provided the spread is profitable.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hyVV!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd616ece5-5d1a-4d8a-b5fd-8a1f5c6e8ceb_3939x1740.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!hyVV!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fd616ece5-5d1a-4d8a-b5fd-8a1f5c6e8ceb_3939x1740.png)

notes: S/ACS = repos executed through the FICC’s Sponsored Repo and Agent Clearing Services (ACS)

Beyond the triparty market lie rates traded in [bilateral](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/bilateral-repos) segments: [DVP Repo and NCCBR](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part-93d). The vast majority of volumes here involve dealers facilitating [basis trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/basis-trades) that keep UST cash and futures prices efficient and [RV (relative value) trades](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-bilateral-repo-markets?utm_source=publication-search) that exploit perceived mispricings between similar UST issues, indirectly boosting efficiency and liquidity in U.S. sovereign debt markets.

The o/n DVP rate reflects the price at which dealers and banks (FICC members) trade [bilateral](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/bilateral-repos) repos among themselves and sponsor, or act as agents for, their clients (without FICC membership) to enter leveraged trades on a centrally cleared basis. [Sponsored Repo](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/sponsored-membership) (Sponsored DVP) and [Agent Clearing Service](https://www.dtcc.com/clearing-and-settlement-services/ficc-gov/agent-clearing-service) (DVP ACS) legs, denoted as DVPS/ACS, trade at a slight premium to other o/n DVP trades, producing wider spreads than those in the interdealer market.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gSrS!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa0e98df8-bf35-4f29-b8cf-0bd4f45518e1_3923x1731.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!gSrS!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fa0e98df8-bf35-4f29-b8cf-0bd4f45518e1_3923x1731.png)

notes: S/ACS = repos executed through the FICC’s Sponsored Repo and Agent Clearing Services (ACS)

Trading through the FICC also increases costs and reduces potential leverage. The demand for lower margins and haircuts has forced dealers to finance most speculative trades outside the FICC in the uncleared DVP market: [NCCBR](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/demystifying-the-repo-market-part-93d) (non-centrally cleared bilateral repo).

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Bb39!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2c4c4be7-c62c-4cf2-9518-1ac48dd39099_3956x1222.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!Bb39!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2F2c4c4be7-c62c-4cf2-9518-1ac48dd39099_3956x1222.png)

Approximately 70% of volume in the NCCBR market involves hedge funds going long one Treasury and short another on leverage, such as trading the relative value between an [on-the-run](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/on-the-runtreasuries.asp) and [off-the-run](https://www.investopedia.com/terms/o/off-the-runtreasuries.asp) Treasury. With scant regulatory oversight, dealers compete for business by lowering margins and haircuts, allowing RV funds to achieve the highest possible leverage. Hedge funds also use NCCBR to finance basis trades by borrowing cash from dealers via NCCBR, DVP Repo, GCF, and triparty. The other ~30% of volumes involves dealers financing leveraged asset purchases for other financial entities, such as mortgage REITs, pension funds, and insurance companies. The average bid and ask on these trades form the o/n NCCBR rate, the most expensive in the private repo rate universe.

Regardless, o/n NCCBR is an uncleared rate and will soon lose its prestige once the SEC’s clearing mandate goes live. DVP Repo and its Sponsored Repo and ACS “offspring” will absorb trillions in speculative volumes fleeing from the NCCBR market, an exodus set to alter where Fed benchmarks print. The Fed’s [TGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr) (the Triparty General Collateral Rate) most closely mirrors o/n TPR, tracking rates on trades in the triparty segment. [BGCR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/bgcr), the Broad General Collateral Rate, measures a broader set of triparty trades (hence the name) and captures both interdealer and regular triparty rates — the average of o/n TPR and o/n GCF. [SOFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/sofr), the Secured Overnight Financing Rate, the U.S. central bank’s major repo benchmark, captures rates on o/n trades in both funding (triparty/GCF) and speculative (DVP) segments, measuring the average of a trio: [o/n TPR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-tpr-overnight-triparty), [o/n GCF](http://o/n%20GC), and [o/n DVP](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-dvp-overnight-dvp). Presently untracked by the U.S. regulatory complex and “under attack” from the centrally-cleared evolution, [o/n NCCBR](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-nccbr-overnight-nccbr), the funding rate underpinning most leveraged trades, will soon be superseded by o/n DVP and its minions[25](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-25-186621587).

Together, TGCR, BGCR, and SOFR comprise the Fed’s secured rate complex, with one of these set to become the next target rate. The hard part will be to choose which, if any, one underlies the U.S. central bank’s primary tool in the modern era. If directed, the Fed’s architects shall engage in target-rate alchemy, reopening their workshop to construct a fresh benchmark. As is now evident, they possess numerous elements to sculpt a new creation.

Despite a range of options to replace [EFFR](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/effr), the Fed will choose the easiest approach, selecting a target from the trio of benchmarks within the secured rate complex. Each rate outshines the U.S. central bank’s existing target across all metrics. Subsequently, the inner workings of each Fed benchmark, now unveiled, should determine the victor and, in turn, how markets react to the new “divine rate” the Fed chooses. In the most likely scenario, Fed officials will, within two years, declare the start of the official transition away from targeting rates based on [arbitrage](https://pro.conks.plumbing/p/pro-infographics-the-fed-funds-arbitrage) among a select few banks, EFFR, to rates based on [secured funding](https://www.newyorkfed.org/markets/reference-rates/tgcr) in repo markets: TGCR.

Officials should quickly rule out BGCR, the Broad General Collateral Rate, which has redundant features relative to its counterparts. TGCR captures over a trillion dollars in “client-to-dealer” repo trades — i.e. secured cash lending to dealers by money funds. BGCR captures the same client-to-dealer trades but adds on a mere $100 billion in dealer-to-dealer volume, trades also exposed to volatility as dealers tidy their balance sheets before reporting to both regulators and their internal risk departments. Luckily, the Fed uses a volume-weighted mean in its benchmark calculation, which masks the small number of volumes traded at extreme outliers. This keeps TGCR and BGCR printing at nearly identical levels. Nevertheless, BGCR introduces noise that the Fed won’t want to embed into its new target[26](https://substack.com/home/post/p-186621587#footnote-26-186621587).

Monetary leaders require a benchmark that provides a clear measure of o/n repo rates, the most accurate gauge of dollar funding. TGCR, which measures trillions of dollars in repos between [dealer banks](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/dealer-banks) and the widest range of private-sector counterparties (without introducing the volatility of dealer markets), remains the ideal choice. News of the Fed selecting TGCR will not only help to quash money market volatility but reinforce the U.S. central bank’s _Great Compression,_ keeping its benchmarks printing well within their [o/n sweet spot](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/infographics-the-feds-repo-rate-sweet). The magnitude of this volatility crush and subsequent market moves will be the puzzle that traders must solve.

[

![](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!A9ZI!,w_1456,c_limit,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb8dfbf13-bc89-4ee8-87a6-0e62720ca540_3965x1764.png)

](https://substackcdn.com/image/fetch/$s_!A9ZI!,f_auto,q_auto:good,fl_progressive:steep/https%3A%2F%2Fsubstack-post-media.s3.amazonaws.com%2Fpublic%2Fimages%2Fb8dfbf13-bc89-4ee8-87a6-0e62720ca540_3965x1764.png)

What’s more, it’s not over for SOFR. Unless central bankers favor monetary alchemy and create a fresh benchmark, Fed architects will likely embark on a two-step target rate evolution: first targeting TGCR, then switching to targeting SOFR. The first step allows the U.S. central bank to enter a holding period until major repo market transitions are complete — namely, the central clearing mandate and the Fed centrally clearing its o/n repo operations (a.k.a [overnight SRPs](https://www.conks.plumbing/i/178410557/on-srps-aka-overnight-fed-repos)). Only then will SOFR be ready for the limelight. Officials won’t want a benchmark that, even in an abundant reserve regime, prints regularly outside the Fed’s [target band](https://www.conks.plumbing/p/glossary?open=false#%C2%A7target-band), the sole scenario where plumbing overrides the macro, and officials must intervene. Targeting SOFR straight away will signal to the market that the Fed is keen to compress rates and supply liquidity at a much greater level.

Instead, the Fed should wait. Both the centrally cleared transition and a cleared Fed will take time and cause pain to implement, but should compress repo rates enough for the Fed to easily target SOFR. The road to a SOFR target rate, among other enhancements, will be long and windy, with a Warsh-led central bank potentially slowing implementation further. Even so, the transition will ultimately begin. After all, with the decaying state of the interbank market and o/n FF, the clock is ticking.
